Killing Harmful Animals in Ihram: No Compensation for the Seven Fawasiq
Ruling on killing harmful animals by muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing harmful animals while in ihram, identify the seven fawasiq from the hadith, and explain why no compensation is due.

Core Idea
Killing the five (or seven) harmful animals is mubah without penalty, even for a muhrim.

This passage clarifies the Hanafi position on killing certain harmful animals (fawasiq) while in the state of ihram. The matn states that no compensation (jaza) is due for killing crows, kites, wolves, snakes, scorpions, mice, and vicious dogs. The proof is the famous hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari listing five animals, with additions from Sunan Abi Dawud and al-Tahawi. The commentator explains the scope and discusses intra-madhhab differences, especially regarding the wild beast and the type of crow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is killing certain harmful animals. The ruling is that no compensation is due—the act is mubah (permissible) even during ihram.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The primary proof is the hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'Five types of animals there is no sin on the one who kills them...' The phrase 'لا حرج' (no sin/liability) indicates permissibility and absence of compensation.
The hadith is ahad in transmission (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
3
Note the expansion from five to seven
The author includes the snake and wolf because of additional narrations in Sunan Abi Dawud (snake) and al-Tahawi (wolf). This is a legitimate expansion within the Hanafi school.
The term 'fasiq' (sinful) is applied due to their harmful nature.
4
Understand the scope and applicability
The ruling applies equally to a muhrim and a non-muhrim inside the Haram. The author also includes other small pests (mosquitoes, ants, etc.) in the same category, though they are not in the hadith.
Some later scholars included these by qiyas (analogy) due to their harm.
5
Recognize intra-Hanafi disagreements
Abu Yusuf restricts the crow to carrion-eaters only. There is also a dispute about the wild beast (sabu'): some treat it as game requiring compensation, others include it under the hadith if it attacks.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the seven specific animals; for the beast, the ruling depends on whether it attacks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا شيء بقتل غراب وحدأة وذئب وحية وعقرب وفأرة وكلب عقور وبعوض ونمل وبرغوث وقراد وسلحفاة أما الفواسق وهي السبعة المذكورة هنا فلما في صحيح البخاري «خمس من الدواب لا حرج على من قتلهن الغراب والحدأة والفأرة والعقرب والكلب العقور ) وزاد في سنن أبي داود الحية والسبع العادي» وفي رواية الطحاوي «الذئب» فلذا ذكر المصنف سبعة، ومعنى الفسق فيهن خبثهن وكثرة الضرر فيهن، وهو حديث مشهور فلذا خص به الكتاب القطعي . كذا في النهاية. وأطلق المصنف في نفي شيء بقتلها فأفاد أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون محرماً أو حلالاً في الحرم وأطلق في الغراب فشمل غير كراهة قوله : (بخلاف) جنين (المرأة) أي حرة أو أمة إذا خرج ميتاً أي وماتت الأم بعده ولهذا عبر في المعراج بقوله : ثم ماتت الأم. وقوله لا يلزم الضارب شيئاً صوابه شيء» ومعناه لا يلزمه الدية كما يلزمه دية الأم أو قيمتها لو أمة وإلا فالغرة لازمة. واحترز بقوله إذا خرج ميتاً» عما إذا خرج حياً فمات فإن فيه الدية كاملة وأما إن ماتت فألقته ميتاً فدية الأم فقط قوله : (فلذا ذكر المصنف سبعة) وإنما لم يذكر السبع مع أنه مذكور في رواية أبي داود لأنه صيد عندنا فيجب فيه الجزاء، أو لأنه قيده بالعادي وسيذكره بقوله وإن صال لا شيء بقتله بقي الكلام في عدم عده منها وجعله من الصيود على ما هو ظاهر الرواية وللمحقق في الفتح كلام أطال البحث فيه وقال . في آخره: ولعل لعدم قوة وجهه كان في السباع روايتان قوله : ( ففيه نظر رده في النهر بما في البدائع. وقال أبو يوسف: الغراب المذكور في الحديث الذي يأكل الجيف أو يخلط لأن هذا النوع هو الذي يبتدئ
His saying: (And nothing is due for killing a crow, a kite, a wolf, a snake, a scorpion, a mouse, a vicious dog, mosquitoes, ants, fleas, ticks, and a turtle. As for the 'fasiq' animals, which are the seven mentioned here, because in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'Five types of animals there is no sin on the one who kills them: the crow, the kite, the mouse, the scorpion, and the vicious dog.' And in Sunan Abi Dawud he added: 'the snake and the aggressive beast.' And in a narration of al-Tahawi: 'the wolf.' So the author mentioned seven. The meaning of 'fisq' in them is their evil and abundance of harm. It is a famous hadith, therefore the decisive book is specified with it. As in al-Nihaya. And the author was absolute in negating anything for killing them, so it indicates that there is no difference between a muhrim and a non-muhrim in the Haram. And he was absolute in the crow, so it includes, without dislike, his saying: (contrary to the human fetus) i.e., a free woman or a slave, if it emerges dead, i.e., and the mother dies after it, and hence he expressed in al-Mi'raj with his words: then the mother died. And his saying 'the striker is not liable for anything' – the correct word is 'nothing' – meaning he is not liable for the blood money (diya) as he is liable for the mother's diya or her value if a slave, otherwise a slave's blood money is obligatory. And he restricted by his saying 'if it emerges dead' contrasting with if it emerges alive and then dies, for then the full diya is due. And if she dies and then aborts it dead, only the mother's diya. His saying: (hence the author mentioned seven) and he did not mention the beast (sabu') although it is mentioned in the narration of Abu Dawud because according to us it is game, so a penalty is due, or because he restricted it to the aggressive one and will mention it in his saying 'and if it attacks, nothing is due for killing it'. There remains discussion on not counting it among them and considering it as game according to the apparent narration. And the investigator in al-Fath has a lengthy discussion and said at its end: perhaps due to lack of strength of its aspect, there are two narrations concerning beasts. His saying: (there is consideration) it is refuted in al-Nahr by what is in al-Bada'i. And Abu Yusuf said: The crow mentioned in the hadith is the one that eats carrion or mixes, because that type is the one that begins.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage expands on the matn ruling that a muhrim may kill certain harmful animals without any compensation (jaza). The author lists seven types (crow, kite, wolf, snake, scorpion, mouse, vicious dog) and adds more (mosquitoes, ants, fleas, ticks, turtle). The proof is the famous hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari listing five, with additions from other sources. The term 'fasiq' (sinful) applied to these animals indicates their harmful nature. The ruling applies equally inside the Haram sanctuary and whether the killer is in ihram or not. The commentator then distinguishes between the animal fetus case and the human fetus case: for a human fetus, if the mother dies first, no diya is due for the fetus alone, only the mother's diya. This is because the human fetus is not a separate soul in the eyes of financial penalties (gharamat), whereas for game, the fetus is treated as a separate entity by precaution (ihtiyat). The discussion also involves a dispute about whether a wild beast (sabu') is included; Abu Yusuf restricts the crow to carrion-eaters. The author notes that some later scholars (al-Fath, al-Nahr) debate the inclusion of the beast, with some narrations treating it as game requiring penalty. The relied-upon position is that the seven mentioned are free from compensation, while others (like the beast) may be subject to rules of game.

Key Terms
فواسق (fawasiq): The five or seven animals that are considered harmful and sinful (fasiq) to kill even in ihram; the term comes from the hadith.
الغراب (al-ghurab): The crow; Abu Yusuf specifies it as the carrion-eating crow that mixes filth. There is ikhtilaf on which type is meant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Five
B) Seven
C) Three
D) Nine

Key Takeaway
Killing the five (or seven) fawasiq in ihram is permissible without compensation, as established by the explicit hadith and supported by Hanafi usul.

Ruling on Killing Harmful Animals (Fawasiq) in Ihram
Listing animals exempt from penalty and explaining hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the seven fawasiq exempt from penalty when killed by a muhrim, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the crow and wolf.

Core Idea
A muhrim is exempt from penalty for killing harmful animals (fawasiq) as per hadith, but the exact list is subject to scholarly dispute even within the Hanafi school.

One of the leniencies granted to a muhrim is the permission to kill certain harmful animals without incurring any penalty. This ruling is based on a well-known hadith that lists five animals, later expanded by other narrations. However, the precise scope of these animals—especially the crow and wolf—has been debated among Hanafi scholars. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its evidence, and the internal disagreements within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its source
Find the statement in the passage that says 'There is nothing for killing...' and note the list of animals. Recognize that this is the ruling being commented upon.
The ruling appears in the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq and is explained by al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.
2
List the seven fawasiq and their evidence
Extract the seven animals explicitly mentioned: crow, kite, wolf, snake, scorpion, mouse, vicious dog. Then identify the hadith from Bukhari and the additional narrations from Abu Dawud and al-Tahawi that form the evidence.
The author mentions seven because of al-Tahawi's inclusion of the wolf.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the crow
Note that Abu Yusuf restricts the crow to the type that eats carrion or initiates harm, while the general term includes all crows. Understand the reasoning: only the harmful type is meant, as it is consistent with the meaning of fisq.
The magpie is considered a separate case; the apparent narration is that it is game (sayd).
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: if a muhrim kills a wolf, he has no penalty according to the Hanafi position that includes the wolf; if he kills a magpie, he may owe compensation because it is considered game.
The mufta bihi is to include the wolf and to consider the magpie as sayd.

فصل إن قتل محرم صيداً. حراماً لا يلزمه شيء. وعلل له في المحيط بأنه لا يفتقر إلى الذكاة فلا يصير ميتة، ولهذا يباح أكل البيض قبل الشيء . وأفاد بمسألة خروج الفرخ أنه لو ضرب بطن ظبية فألقيت جنيناً ميتاً فإنه يضمن قيمته حياً، فإن ماتت الأم ضمن قيمتها أيضاً بخلاف جنين المرأة إذا خرج ميتاً لا يلزم الضارب شيئاً لأنه في حكم النفس في جزاء الصيد احتياطاً وفي حقوق العباد في حكم الجزء لأن غرامات الأموال لا تبتنى على الاحتياط كذا في النهاية. وقيد بقوله «به» لأنه لو علم موته بغير الكسر فلا ضمان عليه للفرخ لانعدام الإمانة ولا للبيض لعدم العرضية، وإذا ضمن الفرخ لا يجب في البيض شيء لأن ما ضمانه لأجله قد ضمنه. وأشار بخروج الفرخ إلى أنه لو نفر صيداً عن بيضة ففسد أنه يضمن إحالة للفساد عليه لأنه السبب الظاهر كما لو أخذ بيضة الصيد فدفنها تحت دجاجة، ففسدت، ولو لم تفسد وخرج منها فرخ وطار فلا شيء عليه .

قوله: (والسلحفاة بضم الحاء وفتح الفاء) كذا في بعض النسخ وكأنها من تحريف النساخ، والأصل وفتح اللام، وفي بعضها بضم الفاء وفتح العين أي فاء الكلمة السين وعينها وهي السلام قوله : (فعليه الجزاء لو وضع ثيابه في الشمس ليقتل الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية: وفي شرح
Chapter: If a muhrim kills game. Haram? nothing is incumbent upon him. It is explained in al-Muhit that it does not require slaughter, so it does not become carrion, and for this reason eating the egg before something is permissible. And he benefited with the issue of the chick's emergence that if he struck the belly of a gazelle and she dropped a dead fetus, then he guarantees its value alive; if the mother dies, he also guarantees her value, unlike the fetus of a woman if it comes out dead – the striker is not liable for anything because it is in the ruling of a life in the penalty of game as a precaution, and in the rights of servants it is in the ruling of a part because monetary compensations are not based on precaution, as in al-Nihaya. And he restricted with his statement 'with it' because if he knew its death without the break, then there is no guarantee upon him for the chick due to the absence of trust, nor for the egg due to the lack of something? And if he guarantees the chick, nothing is required for the egg because what is guaranteed for its sake has been guaranteed. And he indicated with the chick's emergence that if he scared a mother bird from its egg and it spoiled, he guarantees it, attributing the spoilage to him because he is the apparent cause, as if he took the egg of a mother bird and buried it under a hen and it spoiled, and if it did not spoil and a chick emerged from it and flew away, then nothing is upon him.
His statement: (And nothing for killing a crow, kite, wolf, snake, scorpion, mouse, vicious dog, mosquito, ant, flea, tick, turtle) As for the vermin (fawasiq), which are the seven mentioned here, because in Sahih al-Bukhari: 'Five animals for which there is no sin upon one who kills them: the crow, the kite, the mouse, the scorpion, and the vicious dog.' And in Sunan Abi Dawud he added the snake and the predatory beast. And in the narration of al-Tahawi: 'the wolf.' So the author mentioned seven. The meaning of transgression (fisq) in them is their wickedness and abundance of harm. And it is a well-known hadith, so he specified with it the definitive book? as in al-Nihaya. And the author was absolute in negating anything for killing them, so he indicated that there is no difference between being muhrim or halal in the Haram. And he was absolute regarding the crow, so it includes other than disliked? His statement: (Unlike) the fetus of a woman i.e., free or slave if it comes out dead, i.e., and the mother dies after it, and for this reason he expressed in al-Mi'raj: 'then the mother died.' And his statement 'the striker is not liable for anything' – its correct form is 'anything' and its meaning is that he is not liable for blood money (diya) just as he is liable for the mother's diya or her value if she is a slave, otherwise, the ghurrah is required. And he guarded with his statement 'if it came out dead' against the case if it came out alive then died, for then there is full diya. And if she died and then dropped it dead, then only the mother's diya. His statement: (So the author mentioned seven) And he did not mention the predatory beast even though it is mentioned in the narration of Abu Dawud because it is game in our school, so compensation is required for it, or because he restricted it with 'predatory' and he will mention it in his statement 'And if it attacks, nothing for killing it.' There remains discussion about not counting it among them and making it among game, which is the apparent narration. And for the investigator in al-Fath there is discussion, he prolonged the research, and said at its end: Perhaps due to the weakness of its evidence, there are two narrations regarding predatory beasts. His statement: (There is consideration) He refuted it in al-Nahr with what is in al-Bada'i. And Abu Yusuf said: The crow mentioned in the hadith is the one that eats carrion or mixes because this type is the one that initiates harm. And he pointed in al-Mi'raj to refuting what is in Ghayat al-Bayan that it does not do that often. And by this the claim of perpetuity is refuted. And since what is consistent is its initiating harm, the second Imam (Abu Yusuf) confined the reasoning to it. Then I saw in al-Zahiriya he said: Regarding the magpie, there are two narrations, and the apparent is that it is from game. End quote. I say: By this it is apparent that what is in al-Hidaya is the apparent narration. His statement: (Because other than the vicious dog is appropriate) And 'because' with a waw is conjunctive on his statement 'following'. His statement: (Because the command to kill dogs was abrogated) As stated in al-Fath. He said in al-Nahr: But I saw in al-Multaqat the following wording: 'If dogs become numerous in a village and harm its people, their owners are commanded to kill them, and if they refuse, the matter is raised to the judge so that he may order that.' So what is in al-Fath is applied when there is no harm. His statement: (And turtle with dammah on the ha and fatha on the fa) is in some copies, and it seems to be from scribal error; the original has fatha on the lam. In some copies there is dammah on the fa and fatha on the 'ayn, meaning the fa of the word is sin and its 'ayn is salam. His statement: (Then he is liable for compensation if he placed his clothes in the sun to kill... etc.) He said in al-Sharnablaliya: And in the commentary.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on a matn (Kanz al-Daqa'iq) dealing with a muhrim killing animals. The passage is of type 'ruling with proof', where the author lists animals whose killing incurs no penalty and supports it with a hadith. The commentary starts with earlier discussion on killing a bird's egg and fetus of a gazelle, noting that if the fetus is dead, the guarantee is of the animal's value, unlike a human fetus where a ghurrah is due because of the difference in legal status. The main ruling states: 'There is nothing for killing crow, kite, wolf, snake, scorpion, mouse, vicious dog, mosquito, ant, flea, tick, turtle.' The author notes that the seven animals mentioned (the fawasiq) are based on a hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari listing five, with additions from Sunan Abi Dawud and al-Tahawi. The wolf is included because of al-Tahawi's narration. The work then discusses why the predatory beast (sabu') is not included: it is considered game (sayd) in the Hanafi school, so killing it requires compensation unless it attacks. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf specifies that the crow meant is the one that eats carrion or initiates harm, while the author of al-Hidaya includes only that type; the commentator notes that the magpie has two narrations. The passage also clarifies that the word 'vicious' for dog is taken from the hadith, but in reality, any dog that is not game requires no compensation if killed. However, killing dogs is generally abrogated except when harmful. The passage ends with linguistic notes on turtle spelling and a reference to another commentary.

Key Terms
الفواسق (al-fawāsiq): Animals that are harmful and wicked; killing them is permissible even in ihram without penalty. The term is derived from fisq (transgression) due to their harm.
الغراب (al-ghurāb): Crow; but there is ikhtilaf on which type is meant: Abu Yusuf specifies the one that eats carrion or mixes (i.e., begins harm). The general term includes all crows, but the restricted view is that only the harmful type is exempt.
الذئب (al-dhi’b): Wolf; included in the list of fawasiq based on a narration of al-Tahawi. Some earlier sources did not include it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the wolf included among the seven fawasiq?
A) It is mentioned in Sahih al-Bukhari
B) It is mentioned in the narration of al-Tahawi
C) It is considered game in the Hanafi school
D) It is a vicious dog

Key Takeaway
Killing harmful animals (fawasiq) while in ihram incurs no penalty, but the exact list is debated; within the Hanafi school, the wolf is included and the crow is restricted to the harmful type per Abu Yusuf.

Classification of Game and Exempted Pests for a Muhrim
Expands the definition of 'sawd' and lists animals exempt as pests.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi classification of game (sayd) and pests (fawasiq) for a Muhrim, stating which animals are exempt from compensation and why.

Core Idea
A Muhrim must compensate for killing game, but harmful pests are exempt by Hadith and analogy.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on a Muhrim killing various animals. The core distinction is between 'sayd' (game) requiring compensation (jaza') and 'fawasiq' (pests) that are exempt because they are harmful. We will follow the argument from Al-Bahr al-Raiq and see how Hanafi scholars applied criteria like 'initial harm' to classify crows, dogs, wolves, and other creatures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The main text states that a Muhrim killing game requires compensation, but certain animals are exempt as pests. Understand that the hukm is wajib for game, no compensation for pests.
The exemption is based on Hadith and the principle of harm.
2
Understand the criterion for exemption
The key is whether the animal 'initiates harm' (yabtadi'u bil-adha ghaliban). If it generally causes harm, it is considered a fasiq (pest) and is exempt.
The Hanafi principle: al-ghalib ka al-muhaqqaq.
3
Apply to specific animals
Animals like the crow that eats carrion, the vicious dog, and the wolf (by preferred view) are exempt. Magpies and non-vicious dogs are not exempt because they do not typically initiate harm.
The matn restricts dogs to 'aqur (vicious) following the Hadith.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam al-Tahawi differed on the wolf, classifying it as game. The majority view (al-Karkhi, al-Hidayah) equates the wolf to the vicious dog. The relied-upon position is the majority view.
The wolf dispute is a clear intra-Hanafi difference.
5
Determine the practical impact
If a Muhrim kills a wolf, no compensation is required (mufta bihi). If one follows al-Tahawi, compensation would be due. Similarly, the wild cat has two narrations.
The mufta bihi for the magpie is that it is considered game (from sayd), so compensation is required.

كتاب الحج فصل إن قتل محرم صيداً
الغراب بأنواعه الثلاثة وما في الهداية من قوله والمراد بالغراب الذي يأكل الجيف أو يخلط يبتدئ بالأذى، أما العقعق غير مستثنى لأنه لا يسمى غراباً ولا يبتدئ بالأذى ففيه نظر لأنه دائماً يقع على دبر الدابة كما في غاية البيان وسوى المصنف بين الذئب والكلب العقور وهو رواية الكرخي واختارها في الهداية لأن الذئب يبتدئ بالأذى غالباً والغالب كالمتحقق، ولأنه ذكر في بعض الروايات، وفرق بينهما الإمام الطحاوي فلم يجعل الذئب من الفواسق . وأطلق في الفأرة فشملت الأهلية والوحشية. وقيد الكلب بالعقور اتباعاً للحديث مع أن العقور وغيره سواء، أهلياً كان أو وحشياً، لأن غير العقور ليس بصيد فلا يجب الجزاء به كما صرح به قاضيخان في فتاواه واختاره في الهداية. وفي السنور البري روايتان. ثم اعلم أن الكلام إنما هو في وجوب الجزاء بقتله. وأما حل القتل فما لا يؤذي لا يحل قتله فالكلب الأهلي إذا لم يكن مؤذياً لا يحل قتله لأن والخنافس والوزغ والحلمة وصياح الليل وابن عرس وينبغي أن يكون العقرب والفأرة من هذا القسم لأن حد الصيد لا يوجد فيهما . والبعوض من صغار البق الواحدة بعوضة بالهاء واشتقاقها من البعض لأنها كبعض البقة قال عقور وبعوض ونمل وبرغوث وقراد وسلحفاة وبقتل قملة وجرادة تصدق بما شاء ولا

متولدة
قوله : ( وبقتل قملة وجرادة تصدق بما شاء أما وجوب الصدقة بقتل القملة فلأنها من النفث الذي على البدن والمحرم ممنوع من إزالته بمنزلة إزالة الشعر حتى لو قتل ما على الأرض من القمل فإنه لا شيء عليه، أو قتلها من بدن غيره فكذلك كما في الظهيرية وغيرها. وفي المحيط ويكره قتل القملة وما تصدق به فهو خير منها. أطلق في قتل القملة فشمل ما إذا كان مباشرة أو تسبباً لكن يشترط في الثاني القصد كما قدمناه فعليه الجزاء لو وضع ثيابه في الشمس ليقتل حر الشمس القمل كالصيد ولا شيء عليه لو لم يقصد ذلك كما لو غسل ثوبه فمات القمل. كذا في غاية البيان. وقد علم من كلامه أن القمل كالصيد فأفاد أن الدلالة موجبة فيها، فلو أشار المحرم إلى قملة على بدنه فقتلها الحلال وجب الجزاء. وعلم من التعليل أن إلقاء القملة كالقتل لأن الموجب إزالتها عن البدن لا خصوص القتل كما صرح به الأسبيجابي وغيره وأراد بالقملة القليل منه لأن الكثير منه جزاء قتله صدقة معينة وهي نصف صاع لا التصدق بما شاء، وظاهر كلام الأسبيجابي أن ما زاد على الثلاث كثير، وكلام قاضيخان أن العشر فما فوقها كثير، واقتصر شراح الهداية على الأول فكان هو المذهب. وأما وجوبها بقتل الجرادة فلأن الجراد من صيد البر فإن الصيد ما لا يمكن أخذه إلا بحيلة ويقصده الآخذ وقال عمر رضي الله عنه : تمرة خير من جرادة فأوجبها على من قتل جرادة

النقاية للبرجندي مثله ثم نقل خلافه عن المنصورية وهو نفي الجزاء قوله : (فلو أشار الخ) وكذا لو قال الحلال ادفع عني هذا القمل أو أمره بقتلها فقتلها (لباب) قال شارحه : وكذا لو دفع ثوبه ليقتل ما فيه ففعل قوله : (وأراد بالقملة الخ) قال في اللباب : إن قتل محرم قملة تصدق بكسرة، وإن كانت ثنتين أو ثلاثاً فقبضة من طعام، وفي الزائد على الثلاث بالغاً ما بلغ نصف صاع ا هـ. قال شارحه : في البدائع والفتح وهو الذي روى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة. وفي الجامع الصغير : في قملة أطعم شيئاً. وهذا يدل على شيء يسير . قال في الذخيرة وهو الأصح ا هـ . ورواية الحسن سيذكرها المؤلف قريباً قوله : (وأما وجوبها بقتل الجرادة الخ) قال في اللباب : ولو وطئ جراداً عامداً أو جاهلاً فعليه الجزاء إلا أن يكون كثيراً قد سد الطريق فلا يضمن ولو شوى جراداً فأكله بعد ما ضمنه لا شيء عليه للأكل ويكره بيعه قبل الضمان ا.هـ. قال شارحه وذكر قاضيخان في شرح الجامع الصغير : محرم قطع شجرة من الحرم أو شوى بيض صيد في الحرم أو غيره أو حلب صيداً أو شوى جراداً فعليه الجزاء في جميع ذلك يعني القيمة، ويكره له بيع هذه الأشياء، فإن باع جاز ويملك ثمنه بخلاف الصيد الذي قتله المحرم لأنه ميتة فلا يجوز بيعها. وإذا ملك الثمن إن شاء جعله في القيمة التي يؤديها ، وإن شاء جعله في غيرها، وللمشتري أن ينتفع بذلك من حيث التناول لأن البيض والجراد لا يحتاج فيه إلى الذكاة والحلال والمحرم فيما لا يحتاج إلى الذكاة سواء، وإنما لا يباح للأول
Book of Hajj, Chapter: If a Muhrim kills game. The crow in its three types, and what is in al-Hidayah from his statement: 'And the intended meaning of crow is that which eats carrion or mixes and begins with harm.' As for the magpie, it is not exempted because it is not called a crow and does not begin with harm, so there is consideration in it because it always lands on the back of an animal as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the author equated between the wolf and the vicious dog, and it is a narration of al-Karkhi which he selected in al-Hidayah because the wolf generally begins with harm, and the general is like the certain. And because it is mentioned in some narrations, and Imam al-Tahawi differentiated between them and did not make the wolf from the pests. And he gave an absolute ruling on the mouse, including domesticated and wild. And he restricted the dog to being vicious following the hadith, though the vicious and other are the same, whether domesticated or wild, because the non-vicious is not game, so compensation is not obligatory as Qadikhan stated in his Fatawa and he chose it in al-Hidayah. And regarding the wild cat, there are two narrations. Then know that the speech is only about the obligation of compensation for killing it. As for the permissibility of killing, that which does not harm is not permissible to kill, so the domesticated dog if it is not harmful is not permissible to kill because the command to kill dogs was abrogated, so the killing is restricted to the occurrence of harm. And as for mosquitoes and the like of earth insects, they are not game at all, even if some of them begin with harm like the flea. And included are the hornet, crab, fly, bug, hedgehogs, beetles, gecko, tick, night-owl, and weasel. And it should be that the scorpion and mouse are from this category because the definition of game does not apply to them. And mosquitoes are small bugs, singular 'ba'oodah' with ha, and its derivation is from 'ba'd' because it is like part of a bug. Allah said: 'Indeed, Allah is not timid to present an example, that of a mosquito.' [Baarah 2:26] as in Diya' al-Huloom. In it: The kite (حدأة) with kasra of ha is a known bird, plural 'hida'. As for the adze (حدأة) with fatha of ha, it is an axe used to chip stones, having two heads. And the wolf (ذئب) with hamza is known, its plural forms are 'adhub', 'adhawb', 'dhiab', and 'dhuban'. It is said: 'tadha'abat al-rih' when the wind comes from every direction, and it is also a name for men, and its diminutive is 'dhuwayb'. And the turtle (سلحفاة) with damma on ha and fatha on fa, singular of 'salahif', from the creatures of water. It is also said its derivation is from 'adhā' (harm). End quote. And in al-Mi'raj he pointed to refuting what is in Ghayat al-Bayan by saying that it does not do that generally. And with this the claim of continuity is refuted. And since the consistent thing is its beginning with harm, the second imam confined himself to that as the reason. Then I saw in al-Zahiriyyah he said: 'Regarding the magpie, there are two narrations, and the apparent is that it is from the game.' I say: And with this it appears that what is in al-Hidayah is the apparent narration. His statement: (Because the non-vicious is appropriate, and because with the waw is a conjunction on his statement 'following'). His statement: (Because the command to kill dogs was abrogated.) So mentioned in al-Fath. In al-Nahr he said: But I saw in al-Multaqat what is its wording: If dogs increase in a village and cause harm to its people, their owners are commanded to kill them, and if they refuse, the matter is raised to the judge until he orders that. End quote. So what is in al-Fath is made to apply when there is no harm. His statement: (And the turtle with damma on ha and fatha on fa) is like this in some copies, and it seems to be from the scribes' corruption, and the original is with fatha on lam. In some copies it is with damma on fa and fatha on 'ayn, i.e., the fa of the word is sin and its 'ayn is the letter 'lam'. His statement: (So he must pay compensation if he placed his clothes in the sun to kill, etc.) said in al-Sharnaballiyyah: And in the commentary...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, deals with the chapter on a Muhrim (one in the state of Ihram for Hajj or Umrah) killing game (sawd). The author, Zayla'i, expands on the rulings of which animals necessitate compensation (jaza') when killed by a Muhrim. The main text (matn) lists: 'vicious dog, mosquito, ant, flea, tick, turtle, and for killing a louse and a locust, one gives charity as he wishes.' The commentary clarifies each item and includes intra-Hanafi differences.

The passage begins by discussing the crow: the generally accepted view distinguishes crows that eat carrion and initiate harm from others like magpies. Some scholars, like the author of Ghayat al-Bayan, argue the magpie is not exempted because it always lands on animal backs, implying harm. However, Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and others consider it exempted. The wolf is compared to a vicious dog; the preferred view (as in Al-Hidayah) is that the wolf is also a pest because it generally initiates harm, even though Imam al-Tahawi differentiated. For mice, the ruling is absolute—both domestic and wild. Dogs are restricted by the description 'vicious' following the hadith that commands killing certain dogs, though non-vicious dogs are not considered game and thus require no compensation if killed. The command to kill dogs is considered abrogated except for harmful ones, as per Ibn al-Humam's commentary in Fath al-Qadir. However, in the presence of harm, the judge may order their killing.

The commentary then lists many insects and small animals that are not considered game (sawd) at all, such as mosquitoes, flies, bugs, etc., even if they cause harm. The definition of sawd is that which cannot be caught except by effort and is intended by the hunter. The turtle is also discussed, with a note on its pronounciation.

Moving to lice and locusts: Killing a louse on one's own body requires charity; the amount depends on the number. For one or two lice, a piece of bread or a handful of food; for more than three, half a sa'. There is a difference among Hanafi scholars: al-Asbijabi considers more than three as many, while Qadikhan considers ten and above as many. The relied-upon view is the first, as per the commentators of Al-Hidayah. The reasoning is that lice are like an impurity on the body, and removing them is akin to removing hair, which is prohibited for a Muhrim. Killing lice indirectly (e.g., by placing clothes in the sun) requires compensation only if intended. Killing lice on the ground or on another's body (without initiating the action) has no compensation. For locusts, they are considered game of the land; killing one requires charity of a date or similar. The hadith of Umar (ra) is cited: 'A date is better than a locust.' If a Muhrim steps on many locusts that block the road, no compensation is due. If one roasts locusts after compensating for them, there is no further penalty for eating, but selling before compensation is makruh (disliked).

The passage also discusses cases where a Halal (non-Muhrim) kills a louse at the indication of a Muhrim: compensation is due on the Muhrim. Similarly, if a Muhrim commands a Halal to kill a louse, the Muhrim is liable. The overall ruling is that lice and locusts are treated like game in terms of compensation, though they are not game themselves. The author ends with a note on the permissibility of selling game items before compensation and the ownership of the price.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game; animals that cannot be caught except by effort and are intended by the hunter; killing them by a Muhrim requires compensation.
فواسق (fawasiq): Pests; animals that are exempt from compensation because they are harmful and the command is to kill them even by a Muhrim.
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation required for killing game or specific items (like lice) while in Ihram; can be monetary, sacrificial animal, or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school in this passage, if a Muhrim kills a wolf, what is the ruling?
A) Compensation is required because the wolf is game.
B) No compensation is required because the wolf is a pest (fasiq) like the vicious dog.
C) Compensation is required only if the wolf was domesticated.
D) No compensation is required because all animals are exempt.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school exempts from compensation any animal that customarily initiates harm, equating it to the pests (fawasiq) mentioned in the hadith.

Compensation (Jaza') for Killing Lice and Locusts While in Ihram
Details the ruling on killing lice and locusts with proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on compensation for a Muhrim killing a louse or locust, including the amount of charity and the intra-Hanafi differences on the number of lice considered 'many'.

Core Idea
Killing a louse or locust while in ihram requires charity; the amount depends on the number of lice, and locusts are treated as game.

This lesson focuses on the specific rulings for lice and locusts mentioned in the matn: 'And for killing a louse and a locust, he gives charity as he wishes.' The commentator unpacks this general statement, explaining the obligation (jaza'), the amounts, and the intra-Hanafi disputes. The proof for locusts is the athar of Umar (ra).

Guided Path
1
State the matn ruling
The matn says: 'And for killing a louse and a locust, he gives charity as he wishes.' This indicates an obligation, but the amount is not specified in the matn.
The commentary elaborates on the details.
2
Understand the basis for lice
Lice are considered like an impurity (nathth) on the body. Removing them is analogous to removing hair, which is prohibited for a Muhrim. Hence, compensation is wajib.
The obligation is based on the principle that the Muhrim must not remove anything from the body.
3
Identify the quantity of charity for lice
For one louse: a piece of bread (kisrah). For two or three: a handful (qabdah) of food. For more than three: half a sa' of food. There is a dispute on the threshold of 'many': al-Asbijabi says more than three, Qadikhan says ten or more. The relied-upon view is more than three.
The text also discusses indirect killing: if a Muhrim places clothes in the sun intending to kill lice, he must compensate; if unintentional, no compensation.
4
Understand the ruling for locusts
Locusts are considered game (sayd) of the land. Killing one locust requires charity. The proof is the athar of Umar (ra): 'A date is better than a locust.' This establishes the obligation.
If locusts are numerous and block the road, no compensation is due.
5
Note additional cases and exceptions
If a Halal (non-Muhrim) kills a louse at the indication or command of a Muhrim, the Muhrim is liable. If a Muhrim roasts locusts after compensating for them, there is no further penalty for eating, but selling before compensation is makruh.
The passage also mentions that if a Muhrim steps on many locusts unintentionally, no compensation is required due to necessity.

قوله : ( وبقتل قملة وجرادة تصدق بما شاء أما وجوب الصدقة بقتل القملة فلأنها من النفث الذي على البدن والمحرم ممنوع من إزالته بمنزلة إزالة الشعر حتى لو قتل ما على الأرض من القمل فإنه لا شيء عليه، أو قتلها من بدن غيره فكذلك كما في الظهيرية وغيرها. وفي المحيط ويكره قتل القملة وما تصدق به فهو خير منها. أطلق في قتل القملة فشمل ما إذا كان مباشرة أو تسبباً لكن يشترط في الثاني القصد كما قدمناه فعليه الجزاء لو وضع ثيابه في الشمس ليقتل حر الشمس القمل كالصيد ولا شيء عليه لو لم يقصد ذلك كما لو غسل ثوبه فمات القمل. كذا في غاية البيان. وقد علم من كلامه أن القمل كالصيد فأفاد أن الدلالة موجبة فيها، فلو أشار المحرم إلى قملة على بدنه فقتلها الحلال وجب الجزاء. وعلم من التعليل أن إلقاء القملة كالقتل لأن الموجب إزالتها عن البدن لا خصوص القتل كما صرح به الأسبيجابي وغيره وأراد بالقملة القليل منه لأن الكثير منه جزاء قتله صدقة معينة وهي نصف صاع لا التصدق بما شاء، وظاهر كلام الأسبيجابي أن ما زاد على الثلاث كثير، وكلام قاضيخان أن العشر فما فوقها كثير، واقتصر شراح الهداية على الأول فكان هو المذهب. وأما وجوبها بقتل الجرادة فلأن الجراد من صيد البر فإن الصيد ما لا يمكن أخذه إلا بحيلة ويقصده الآخذ وقال عمر رضي الله عنه : تمرة خير من جرادة فأوجبها على من قتل جرادة

And it should be that the scorpion and mouse are from this category because the definition of game does not apply to them. And mosquitoes are small bugs, singular 'ba'oodah' with ha, and its derivation is from 'ba'd' because it is like part of a bug. Allah said: 'Indeed, Allah is not timid to present an example, that of a mosquito.' [Baarah 2:26] as in Diya' al-Huloom. In it: The kite (حدأة) with kasra of ha is a known bird, plural 'hida'. As for the adze (حدأة) with fatha of ha, it is an axe used to chip stones, having two heads. And the wolf (ذئب) with hamza is known, its plural forms are 'adhub', 'adhawb', 'dhiab', and 'dhuban'. It is said: 'tadha'abat al-rih' when the wind comes from every direction, and it is also a name for men, and its diminutive is 'dhuwayb'. And the turtle (سلحفاة) with damma on ha and fatha on fa, singular of 'salahif', from the creatures of water. It is also said its derivation is from 'adhā' (harm). End quote. And in al-Mi'raj he pointed to refuting what is in Ghayat al-Bayan by saying that it does not do that generally. And with this the claim of continuity is refuted. And since the consistent thing is its beginning with harm, the second imam confined himself to that as the reason. Then I saw in al-Zahiriyyah he said: 'Regarding the magpie, there are two narrations, and the apparent is that it is from the game.' I say: And with this it appears that what is in al-Hidayah is the apparent narration. His statement: (Because the non-vicious is appropriate, and because with the waw is a conjunction on his statement 'following'). His statement: (Because the command to kill dogs was abrogated.) So mentioned in al-Fath. In al-Nahr he said: But I saw in al-Multaqat what is its wording: If dogs increase in a village and cause harm to its people, their owners are commanded to kill them, and if they refuse, the matter is raised to the judge until he orders that. End quote. So what is in al-Fath is made to apply when there is no harm. His statement: (And the turtle with damma on ha and fatha on fa) is like this in some copies, and it seems to be from the scribes' corruption, and the original is with fatha on lam. In some copies it is with damma on fa and fatha on 'ayn, i.e., the fa of the word is sin and its 'ayn is the letter 'lam'. His statement: (So he must pay compensation if he placed his clothes in the sun to kill, etc.) said in al-Sharnaballiyyah: And in the commentary...
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq, deals with the chapter on a Muhrim (one in the state of Ihram for Hajj or Umrah) killing game (sawd). The author, Zayla'i, expands on the rulings of which animals necessitate compensation (jaza') when killed by a Muhrim. The main text (matn) lists: 'vicious dog, mosquito, ant, flea, tick, turtle, and for killing a louse and a locust, one gives charity as he wishes.' The commentary clarifies each item and includes intra-Hanafi differences.

The passage begins by discussing the crow: the generally accepted view distinguishes crows that eat carrion and initiate harm from others like magpies. Some scholars, like the author of Ghayat al-Bayan, argue the magpie is not exempted because it always lands on animal backs, implying harm. However, Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and others consider it exempted. The wolf is compared to a vicious dog; the preferred view (as in Al-Hidayah) is that the wolf is also a pest because it generally initiates harm, even though Imam al-Tahawi differentiated. For mice, the ruling is absolute—both domestic and wild. Dogs are restricted by the description 'vicious' following the hadith that commands killing certain dogs, though non-vicious dogs are not considered game and thus require no compensation if killed. The command to kill dogs is considered abrogated except for harmful ones, as per Ibn al-Humam's commentary in Fath al-Qadir. However, in the presence of harm, the judge may order their killing.

The commentary then lists many insects and small animals that are not considered game (sawd) at all, such as mosquitoes, flies, bugs, etc., even if they cause harm. The definition of sawd is that which cannot be caught except by effort and is intended by the hunter. The turtle is also discussed, with a note on its pronounciation.

Moving to lice and locusts: Killing a louse on one's own body requires charity; the amount depends on the number. For one or two lice, a piece of bread or a handful of food; for more than three, half a sa'. There is a difference among Hanafi scholars: al-Asbijabi considers more than three as many, while Qadikhan considers ten and above as many. The relied-upon view is the first, as per the commentators of Al-Hidayah. The reasoning is that lice are like an impurity on the body, and removing them is akin to removing hair, which is prohibited for a Muhrim. Killing lice indirectly (e.g., by placing clothes in the sun) requires compensation only if intended. Killing lice on the ground or on another's body (without initiating the action) has no compensation. For locusts, they are considered game of the land; killing one requires charity of a date or similar. The hadith of Umar (ra) is cited: 'A date is better than a locust.' If a Muhrim steps on many locusts that block the road, no compensation is due. If one roasts locusts after compensating for them, there is no further penalty for eating, but selling before compensation is makruh (disliked).

The passage also discusses cases where a Halal (non-Muhrim) kills a louse at the indication of a Muhrim: compensation is due on the Muhrim. Similarly, if a Muhrim commands a Halal to kill a louse, the Muhrim is liable. The overall ruling is that lice and locusts are treated like game in terms of compensation, though they are not game themselves. The author ends with a note on the permissibility of selling game items before compensation and the ownership of the price.

Key Terms
صيد (sayd): Game; animals that cannot be caught except by effort and are intended by the hunter; killing them by a Muhrim requires compensation.
قملة (qamlah): A louse; when killed by a Muhrim on his body, charity is required; different rules for many vs. few.
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation required for killing game or specific items (like lice) while in Ihram; can be monetary, sacrificial animal, or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, how many lice must be killed to require half a sa' of charity?
A) 1
B) 3
C) More than 3
D) 10 and above

Key Takeaway
Lice and locusts are treated like game for compensation; the amount of charity depends on the number for lice and is a date for one locust, based on the practice of Umar (ra).

Rulings on Killing Lice and Locusts by a Muhrim
Commentary expanding the matn ruling on killing lice and locusts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on killing lice and locusts during ihram, apply the threshold for few vs many, and explain the dalil and qiyas underpinning the ruling.

Core Idea
Killing a louse or locust in ihram requires sadaqah — few give as you wish, many require half a sa' of food.

The prohibition against removing filth from the body during ihram extends to killing lice. The Hanafi school analogizes this to game and also addresses locusts. This lesson unpacks the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, clarifying the ruling, its proof, and the practical differences within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Killing a louse (qamlah) or a locust (jarādah) by a person in the state of ihram. What is the hukm? The text states a sadaqah (charity) is wajib.
The obligation applies whether killing is direct or indirect, provided there is intent.
2
Locate the dalil for the locust
What evidence is cited? The action and statement of Umar ibn al-Khattab: 'A date is better than a locust' — imposing a penalty. This occurred in the presence of the Companions, indicating silent consensus (ijma suktuti).
A weak hadith from Abu Hurairah classifying locust as sea game is rejected by Hanafis due to its weak chain.
3
Trace the qiyas for the louse
How is the ruling for lice derived? It is analogized (qiyas) to removing hair: lice are filth on the body, and the Muhrim is prohibited from removing such filth. Thus, killing a louse is like cutting hair, requiring compensation.
The illah (cause) is removal of filth from the body, not specifically killing. Therefore, even throwing the louse off the body incurs the penalty.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the threshold
What constitutes 'few' vs 'many'? Al-Asbijabi says more than three is many; Qadikhan says ten or more. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) adopts more than three as the threshold.
For 'many' (more than three), the sadaqah is a fixed half sa' of food. For 'few' (three or fewer), the Muhrim gives charity as he wishes (e.g., a piece of bread or a handful).
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
What is the operative ruling for fatwa? For both lice and locusts: if three or fewer, give charity as you wish; if more than three, give half a sa' of food. This is the position adopted by the commentators of al-Hidayah.
For a slave, expiation is by fasting (one day) since he cannot give sadaqah.

وإن كانت ثنتين أو ثلاثاً فقبضة من طعام، وفي الزائد على الثلاث بالغاً ما بلغ نصف صاع ا هـ. قال شارحه : في البدائع والفتح وهو الذي روى الحسن عن أبي حنيفة. وفي الجامع الصغير : في قملة أطعم شيئاً. وهذا كتاب الحج/ فصل إن قتل محرم صيداً
يجاوز عن شاة بقتل السبع وإن صال لا شيء بقتله بخلاف المضطر وللمحرم ذبح شاة في
كما رواه مالك في الموطأ وتبعه أصحاب المذاهب. أما ما في سنن أبي داود والترمذي عن أبي هريرة قال : خرجنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في حجة أو غزوة فاستقبلنا رجل من جراد فجعلنا نضربه بأسيافنا وقسينا فقال : كلوه فإنه من صيد البحر. فقد أجاب النووي رحمه الله شرح المهذب بأن الحفاظ اتفقوا على تضعيفه لضعف أبي المُهَزِم وهو بضم الميم وكسر الزاي وفتح الهاء بينهما، واسمه يزيد بن سفيان. وفي رواية لأبي داود عن أبي رافع عن أبي هريرة قال البيهقي وغيره : ميمون غير معروف ا هـ . فليس هنا حديث ثابت فثبت أنه من صيد البر بإيجاب عمر الجزاء فيه بحضرة الصحابة، وقد روى البيهقي بسند صحيح عن ابن عباس أنه قال في الجراد قبضة من طعام. ولم أر من تكلم على الفرق بين الجراد القليل والكثير كالقمل، وينبغي أن يكون كالقمل ففي الثلاث وما دونها يتصدق بما شاء، وفي الأربع فأكثر يتصدق بنصف صاع، وفي المحيط : مملوك أصاب جرادة في إحرامه إن صام يوماً فقد زاد وإن شاء جمعها حتى تصير عدة جرادات فيصوم يوماً ا هـ. وينبغي أن يكون القمل كذلك في حق العبد لما علم أن العبد لا يكفر إلا بالصوم. ثم أطلق المصنف رحمه الله في الصدقة لأنه لم يذكر في ظاهر الرواية مقدارها، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه يطعم في الواحدة كسرة وفي الاثنين أو الثلاثة قبضة من الطعام، وفي الأكثر نصف صاع . كذا ذكره الأسبيجابي .
Book of Hajj / Section: If a Muhrim kills game ... For killing a louse and a locust, he gives charity as he wishes. As for the obligation of charity for killing a louse, it is because it is from the filth on the body, and the Muhrim is prohibited from removing it, akin to removing hair. Even if he kills what is on the ground from lice, there is nothing on him, or if he kills it from another's body, similarly. In al-Muhit, it is disliked to kill a louse, and what he gives in charity is better than it. The ruling is general for killing a louse whether directly or by causation, but the second requires intent as we have mentioned. So there is a penalty if he places his clothes in the sun to kill lice by the sun's heat, like game, and nothing on him if he did not intend that, like if he washed his garment and the lice died. It is known from his speech that lice are like game, so indicating that mediation obligates it. So if a Muhrim points to a louse on his body and a non-Muhrim kills it, the penalty is due. From the reasoning, throwing away the louse is like killing, because what is obligatory is its removal from the body, not specifically killing, as al-Asbijabi and others stated. By 'louse' he means the few, because for many, the penalty is a specific charity, which is half a sa', not charity as he wishes. The apparent statement of al-Asbijabi is that what exceeds three is many, and the statement of Qadikhan is that ten and above is many. The commentators of al-Hidayah restricted to the first, so it is the madhhab. As for the obligation for killing a locust, it is because the locust is from land game, for game is what cannot be taken except by stratagem and the taker intends it. Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) said: 'A date is better than a locust,' and he imposed it on whoever killed a locust. Similarly, al-Nuqaya al-Birjandi mentioned it, then transmitted a contrary opinion from al-Mansuriyya which negates the penalty. Then the hadith from Abu Dawood and al-Tirmidhi from Abu Hurairah is discussed and deemed weak. Thus it is established that it is from land game by Umar's imposition of penalty in the presence of the Companions. Ibn 'Abbas said regarding a locust: a handful of food. The author did not see anyone speak on the difference between few and many locusts like lice, and it should be like lice: for three or less, he gives charity as he wishes; for four or more, half a sa'. The author then discusses the slave's expiation by fasting. Then he mentions the riwayah of Hasan from Abu Hanifah regarding the amount for lice: for one, a piece of bread; for two or three, a handful; for more, half a sa'.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding the ruling that a Muhrim who kills a louse (qamla) or a locust (jarada) must give sadaqah (charity) as he wishes. The commentary first explains the obligation for killing a louse. The reason given is that a louse is filth on the body (nathath), and the Muhrim is prohibited from removing such filth, similar to the prohibition of removing hair. Therefore, killing a louse is akin to cutting hair in terms of violating ihram. The obligation applies whether the killing is direct or indirect (e.g., placing clothes in the sun to kill lice), but indirect killing requires intent. If the Muhrim does not intend to kill, there is no penalty. The commentary then establishes that lice are analogous to game (sayd) in that pointing to a louse for a non-Muhrim to kill incurs a penalty. The obligation is to remove the louse from the body, not specifically to kill it. The commentary then addresses the quantity: for few lice (three or less), the Muhrim gives charity as he wishes; for many (more than three), the penalty is a fixed half sa' of food. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: al-Asbijabi considers more than three as many, Qadikhan considers ten and above as many, but the relied-upon position (as per commentators of al-Hidayah) is that three is the threshold.

The commentary then discusses the obligation for killing a locust. The locust is considered land game (sayd al-barr) because it is taken by stratagem. The proof is the action of Umar ibn al-Khattab (radiyallahu 'anhu) who imposed a penalty of a date (tamrah) for each locust, and this was done in the presence of the Companions, constituting a form of ijma'. The commentary then refutes a weak hadith from Abu Hurairah that classifies locust as sea game, citing the consensus of hadith critics that the narration is weak. Thus, the Hanafi position is that killing a locust requires sadaqah. The amount by analogy to lice: for three or less, charity as wished; for four or more, half a sa'. The commentary also mentions that for a slave who kills a locust, expiation is by fasting (since a slave cannot give sadaqah), and he may fast one day or gather multiple locusts to fast one day. The passage concludes by presenting the riwayah of Hasan from Abu Hanifah on the specific amounts for lice: one louse = a piece of bread, two or three = a handful, more than three = half a sa'. This is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
قملة (qamlah): A louse (singular); the text discusses killing one or many on the body of a Muhrim.
جرادة (jarādah): A locust (singular); the text classifies it as land game (sayd al-barr) for which a Muhrim must give sadaqah if killed.
صدقة (sadaqah): Charity given as expiation; here it is a wajib compensation for violating ihram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Half a sa' of food
B) Charity as he wishes (e.g., a piece of bread or a handful)
C) Nothing, because it is insignificant
D) A sheep

Key Takeaway
Killing a louse or locust in ihram requires sadaqah — few (≤3) give as you wish, many (>3) give half a sa' of food.

Penalty for Killing a Predatory Animal in Ihram
Penalty rule for killing a sabu' in ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the penalty for a Muhrim killing a predatory animal, distinguish between an attacking and non-attacking animal, and apply the Hanafi definition of sabu'.

Core Idea
A Muhrim pays one sheep for killing a predatory animal unless it attacks.

In ihram, killing land game is prohibited and incurs a penalty. But what about predatory animals that may harm the pilgrim? This lesson clarifies that the penalty is limited to one sheep, and only if the animal does not attack. If it attacks, no penalty applies because it is then treated as a pest (fasq). We also explore the definition of sabu' and why the wolf is excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled on? Killing a predatory animal (sabu') while in ihram. What is the ruling? A penalty of one sheep is wajib.
The text says 'لا يجاوز عن شاة' meaning the penalty does not exceed a sheep.
2
Distinguish between the two scenarios
If the animal attacks (صال), then no penalty. If it does not attack, a sheep is due. Understand the condition: the animal's behavior determines the ruling.
The commentary explains that the sabu' is not from the foasiq unless it attacks.
3
Define sabu' and its scope
The text defines sabu' as every animal whose meat is not eaten, including lions, tigers, etc. The wolf is excluded because it is from the foasiq (plunders).
The commentator references al-Nahr for the broad definition.
4
Differentiate from the compelled person
The text mentions 'بخلاف المضطر' – unlike the one compelled. The compelled person (e.g., starving) may kill game without penalty. This is a separate ruling.
The passage uses this to highlight that the sabu' ruling is specific and not based on necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يجاوز عن شاة بقتل السبع وإن صال لا شيء بقتله بخلاف المضطر) لأن السبع صيد وليس هو من الفواسق لأنه لا يبتدئ بالأذى حتى لو ابتدأ بالأذى كان منها فلا يجب بقتله شيء وهو معنى قوله صال» أي وثب بخلاف الذئب فإنه من الفواسق لأنه ينتهب. قال في النهر: فكان عدم التخصيص أولى إذ المفهوم معتبر في الروايات اتفاقاً ومنه أقوال
قوله : (وأراد بالسبع كل حيوان لا يؤكل لحمه الخ) قال في النهر : فكان عدم التخصيص أولى إذ المفهوم معتبر في الروايات اتفاقاً ومنه أقوال
His saying: (And he does not exceed a sheep for killing the predatory animal, but if it attacks, there is nothing for killing it, unlike the one compelled). Because the predatory animal is game and is not from the pests because it does not initiate harm. So if it initiates harm, then it is from them (the pests), so nothing is obligatory for killing it. That is the meaning of his saying 'attacks' i.e., leaps. Unlike the wolf, for it is from the pests because it plunders. He said in al-Nahr: So non-specification is more appropriate, for the understood meaning is considered in the narrations unanimously, and from it are sayings.
His saying: (And he meant by predatory animal every animal whose meat is not eaten...) He said in al-Nahr: So non-specification is more appropriate, for the understood meaning is considered in the narrations unanimously, and from it are sayings.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for a Muhrim who kills a predatory animal (sabu'). The matn states: 'ولا يجاوز عن شاة بقتل السبع' meaning the penalty does not exceed one sheep (i.e., it is a sheep, not more). If the animal attacks (صال), then there is no penalty because it becomes like a pest (fasq). The commentary clarifies the reasoning: the sabu' is considered game (sayd) and is not from the foasiq (pests) because it does not initiate harm. However, if it does initiate harm (by attacking), then it is as if it is from the pests, and killing it is permissible without penalty. The wolf (dhi'b) is explicitly excluded as it is always from the pests because it plunders (yantahib). The commentator then references al-Nahr, noting that the definition of sabu' should not be restricted to specific animals; rather, it includes every animal whose meat is not eaten, based on the understanding of the narrations. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that the ruling applies to any harmful predatory animal, not just certain species. The key point is the distinction between a predator that attacks (no penalty) and one that does not (penalty of a sheep).

Key Terms
سبع (sabu'): A predatory animal that does not initiate harm; includes any animal whose meat is not eaten, such as lions, tigers, etc.
صال (sāla): Attacks; if the predator attacks the Muhrim, killing it incurs no penalty.
فواسق (fawāsiq): Pests or harmful animals that can be killed even in ihram without penalty; includes the wolf and similar.
شاة (shāt): A sheep, as the required penalty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the penalty for a Muhrim who kills a lion that is not attacking?
A) No penalty, because it is a pest
B) One sheep
C) Half a sa' of food
D) A cow

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim must pay one sheep for killing a predator that does not attack; if it attacks, no penalty.

Compensation for Killing Game in Ihram: Predatory Animals and Locusts
Expands rulings for muhrim killing sabu' and locusts with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on compensation for a muhrim killing a sabu' (predatory animal) and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute over locusts.

Core Idea
Killing a sabu' by a muhrim incurs compensation capped at a sheep; locusts are land game requiring fidyah, contrary to the Shafi'i view.

This lesson explores the rulings for a muhrim who kills game, focusing on predatory animals (sabu') and locusts. The Hanafi school distinguishes between harmful animals (fawasiq) that may be killed without penalty, and other game that requires compensation. A key inter-madhhab dispute centers on whether locusts are land or sea game, affecting the obligation of fidyah. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the rules of ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of animals
First, distinguish between fawasiq (harmful animals that initiate harm) and sabu' (predatory/inedible animals that do not initiate). Only sabu' requires compensation when killed by a muhrim.
Fawasiq include the wolf, lion, leopard, crow, kite, scorpion, rat, and dog. All others that are not eaten and not harmful in that sense are sabu'.
2
Understand the cap on compensation for sabu'
The penalty for killing a sabu' does not exceed a sheep. The reason is that the value is based only on potential skin and meat, which rarely exceeds a sheep's value. Combativeness and harm are not compensated.
Exceptions: owned sabu' requires full value to owner plus the cap for Allah; if the animal attacks first, no penalty.
3
Analyze the locust dispute
Follow the Hanafi evidence: 'Umar imposed compensation in the presence of Companions (mursal hadith). The opposing hadith (Abu Dawood) is declared weak by al-Nawawi. Thus locusts are land game.
The Shafi'i position relies on the weak hadith. The practical difference is whether fidyah is due.
4
Derive the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: muhrim killing locusts must give charity (handful or half sa'). Shafi'i: no penalty. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi view.
The intra-Hanafi details: one locust = piece of bread, two/three = handful, more = half sa'.

كما رواه مالك في الموطأ وتبعه أصحاب المذاهب. أما ما في سنن أبي داود والترمذي عن أبي هريرة قال : خرجنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم في حجة أو غزوة فاستقبلنا رجل من جراد فجعلنا نضربه بأسيافنا وقسينا فقال : كلوه فإنه من صيد البحر. فقد أجاب النووي رحمه الله شرح المهذب بأن الحفاظ اتفقوا على تضعيفه لضعف أبي المُهَزِم وهو بضم الميم وكسر الزاي وفتح الهاء بينهما، واسمه يزيد بن سفيان. وفي رواية لأبي داود عن أبي رافع عن أبي هريرة قال البيهقي وغيره : ميمون غير معروف ا هـ . فليس هنا حديث ثابت فثبت أنه من صيد البر بإيجاب عمر الجزاء فيه بحضرة الصحابة، وقد روى البيهقي بسند صحيح عن ابن عباس أنه قال في الجراد قبضة من طعام. ولم أر من تكلم على الفرق بين الجراد القليل والكثير كالقمل، وينبغي أن يكون كالقمل ففي الثلاث وما دونها يتصدق بما شاء، وفي الأربع فأكثر يتصدق بنصف صاع، وفي المحيط : مملوك أصاب جرادة في إحرامه إن صام يوماً فقد زاد وإن شاء جمعها حتى تصير عدة جرادات فيصوم يوماً ا هـ. وينبغي أن يكون القمل كذلك في حق العبد لما علم أن العبد لا يكفر إلا بالصوم. ثم أطلق المصنف رحمه الله في الصدقة لأنه لم يذكر في ظاهر الرواية مقدارها، وفي رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة أنه يطعم في الواحدة كسرة وفي الاثنين أو الثلاثة قبضة من الطعام، وفي الأكثر نصف صاع . كذا ذكره الأسبيجابي .
قوله : (ولا يجاوز عن شاة بقتل السبع وإن صال لا شيء بقتله بخلاف المضطر) لأن السبع صيد وليس هو من الفواسق لأنه لا يبتدئ بالأذى حتى لو ابتدأ بالأذى كان منها فلا يجب بقتله شيء وهو معنى قوله صال» أي وثب بخلاف الذئب فإنه من الفواسق لأنه ينتهب. وأراد بالسبع كل حيوان لا يؤكل لحمه مما ليس من الفواسق السبعة والحشرات، سواء كان سبعاً أولا ولو خنزيراً أو قرداً أو فيلاً كما في المجمع والسبع اسم لكل مختطف منتهب جارح قاتل عاد عادة فإذا وجب الجزاء بقتله لا يجاوز به شاة لأن كثرة قيمته إما لما فيه من معنى المحاربة وهو خارج عن معنى الصيدية، أو لما فيه من الإيذاء وهو لا تقوم له شرعاً فبقى اعتبار الجلد واللحم على تقدير كونه مأكولاً وذلك لا يزيد على قيمة الشاة غالباً لأن لحم الشاة خير من لحم السبع. وقيد بالسبع لأن الجمل إذا صال على إنسان فقتله وجب عليه قيمته بالغة ما بلغت والفرق بينهما أن الإذن في مسألة السبع بقتله حاصل من صاحب الحق وهو الشارع . وأما في مسألة الجمل فلم يحصل الإذن من صاحبه، وأورد عليه العبد إذا صال بالسيف على إنسان فقتله المصول عليه فإنه لا يضمنه مع أنه لا إذن له أيضاً من مالكه . وأجيب بأن العبد مضمون في الأصل حقاً لنفسه بالآدمية لا للمولى لأنه مكلف كسائر المكلفين، ألا ترى أنه لو ارتد أو قتل يقتل وإذا كان مضموناً لنفسه سقط هذا الضمان بمبيح جاء من قبله وهو المصال به ومالية المولى فيه إن كانت متقومة مضمونة له نهى تبع النفس فيسقط التبع في ضمن سقوط الأصل. أطلق في عدم وجوب شيء إذا صال فشمل ما إذا أمكنه دفعه بغير سلاح أو لا، وذكر في المحيط أنه إذا أمكنه دفعه بغير السلاح فقتله فعليه الجزاء، وقيد قاضيخان السبع بكونه غير مملوك لأنه لو كان مملوكاً وجبت قيمته بالغة بلغت يعني عليه قيمتان إذا كان محرماً قيمة لمالكه مطلقاً وقيمة الله تعالى لا تجاوز قيمة شاة كما أسلفناه. ومعنى قوله بخلاف المضطر أن المحرم إذا اضطر إلى أكل الصيد للمخمصة فذبحه وأكله فإنه يجب الجزاء عليه لأن الإذن مقيد بالكفارة بالنص في قوله تعالى فمن كان منكم مريضاً الآية. قوله : (ولا يعرف منه مستأنس عندهم الخ
Book of Hajj / Section: If a muhrim kills game... He is not bound to more than a sheep for killing a predatory animal, and if it attacks, nothing is due for killing it, unlike the person forced (by hunger). And for the muhrim, slaughtering a sheep... as narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta, followed by the scholars of the schools. As for what is in Sunan Abi Dawud and al-Tirmidhi from Abu Hurairah who said: We went out with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) on a Hajj or a military expedition, and a large swarm of locusts came towards us, so we started striking them with our swords and bows, and he said: 'Eat it, for it is from the game of the sea.' Al-Nawawi (may Allah have mercy on him) in his commentary on al-Muhadhdhab responded that the hadith scholars agreed to declare it weak due to the weakness of Abu al-Muhzim... So there is no established hadith here, so it is established that it is from land game by the fact that Umar imposed compensation for it in the presence of the Companions. Al-Bayhaqi narrated with a sound chain from Ibn Abbas that he said concerning locusts: 'A handful of food.' And I have not seen anyone discuss the difference between few and many locusts, like lice. It should be like lice: for three or less, he gives charity as he wishes; for four or more, he gives charity half a sa'. In al-Muhit: A slave who catches a locust while in ihram, if he fasts a day he has exceeded, and if he wishes he may collect them until they become several locusts and then fast one day. It should be that lice are the same for a slave because it is known that a slave only makes expiation by fasting. Then the author (may Allah have mercy on him) left the charity unqualified because he did not mention its amount in the apparent narration. In the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa: he feeds for one a piece of bread, for two or three a handful of food, and for more half a sa'. Thus mentioned by al-Asbijabi. His saying: (And he is not bound to more than a sheep for killing a predatory animal, and if it attacks, nothing is due for killing it, unlike the person forced) because the predatory animal is game and it is not among the harmful animals because it does not initiate harm; if it does initiate harm it is among them, so nothing is due for killing it. That is the meaning of his saying 'attacks' – i.e., leaps – unlike the wolf, for it is among the harmful animals because it plunders. And he intended by 'predatory animal' every animal whose meat is not eaten and is not among the seven harmful animals and insects, whether it is a predator or not, even if it is a pig, monkey, or elephant, as in al-Majma'. And 'predatory animal' is a name for every seizing, plundering, tearing, killing, and aggressive by habit. So when compensation is obligatory for killing it, it does not exceed a sheep because the excess of its value is either due to its meaning of combativeness, which is outside the meaning of game, or due to its harmfulness, which is not compensated for legally, so consideration remains for the skin and meat on the assumption it is edible, and that generally does not exceed the value of a sheep because the meat of a sheep is better than the meat of a predatory animal. He restricted it to predatory animal because if a camel attacks a person and he kills it, its full value is due, whatever it may be. The difference between them is that permission to kill the predatory animal comes from the rightful owner, i.e., the Lawgiver. As for the camel, permission has not come from its owner. It is objected against: the slave who attacks a person with a sword, and the attacked person kills him, he is not liable for him, even though he also has no permission from his owner. The answer is that the slave is fundamentally guaranteed as a right for himself due to his humanity, not for his master, because he is accountable like other accountable persons. Do you not see that if he apostatizes or kills, he is executed? And since he is guaranteed for himself, this liability drops due to a permissibility coming from his side, i.e., the attack, and the master's right of financial value, even if it is a measure of value guaranteed for him, is subsidiary to the person, so the subsidiary falls within the fall of the principal. He stated absolutely that nothing is due when it attacks, so it includes whether he could repel it without a weapon or not. In al-Muhit, it is mentioned that if he could repel it without a weapon and kills it, then compensation is due. Qadikhan restricted the predatory animal to being unowned, because if it is owned, its full value is due, meaning two values: for the owner absolutely and the value for Allah that does not exceed a sheep as we have said. And the meaning of his saying 'unlike the person forced' is that when a muhrim is forced to eat game due to hunger, slaughters it and eats it, compensation is due because permission is conditional on expiation by the text of His saying: 'So whoever among you is ill...'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding two main rulings for a muhrim (one in ihram) who kills game. First ruling: killing a predatory animal (sabu'). The matn states that the penalty does not exceed a sheep; if the animal attacks first, no penalty is due. The commentary explains that a predatory animal is not among the fawasiq (harmful animals) because it does not initiate harm. If it does initiate harm, it becomes like the fawasiq, and killing it incurs no penalty. The wolf is excluded from this leniency because it is a fasiq that plunders. The term 'sabu'' is defined broadly: any inedible animal not among the seven fawasiq or insects, including pig, monkey, elephant. The rationale is that the value of such animals is not based on combativeness or harm (which are not compensated), but only on potential skin and meat, which rarely exceed a sheep's value. Exceptions: a camel that attacks requires full payment because no permission from its owner; a slave that attacks is not payable due to his being guaranteed for himself (humanity) and the master's right is subsidiary. If the muhrim can repel without a weapon, he must do so; otherwise, penalty applies. A owned predatory animal requires double compensation: value to owner plus value to Allah capped at a sheep. The forced person (mudtarr) who eats game due to hunger must still pay penalty because permission is tied to expiation (Quran 5:95). Second ruling: locusts (jarad). The text discusses whether locusts are land or sea game. A hadith from Abu Dawood allows eating locusts as sea game, but it is declared weak by al-Nawawi and others. The stronger evidence is 'Umar's imposition of compensation in the presence of Companions, and Ibn 'Abbas's ruling of a handful of food. Thus locusts are land game. The author notes a gap in sources regarding the quantity for few vs. many locusts, analogizing to lice: for three or less, any charity; for four or more, half a sa'. For a slave, only fasting is possible, so similar rules apply. Al-Hasan's narration from Abu Hanifa specifies: one locust → a piece of bread; two or three → handful; more → half sa'. The passage overall demonstrates Hanafi method of relying on sound transmission (mursal of 'Umar) over weak hadith, and using qiyas (analogy) with lice for quantities.

Key Terms
سبع (sabu'): Any predatory animal whose meat is not eaten, excluding the seven fawasiq and insects; used broadly to include pig, monkey, elephant.
فاسق (fasiq (pl. fawasiq)): Harmful animal that initiates harm, e.g., wolf, lion, leopard, etc.; killing it by a muhrim incurs no penalty.
جراد (jarad): Locusts; considered land game by Hanafis, requiring fidyah when killed by muhrim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One cow

D) Nothing at all

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats locusts as land game based on 'Umar's practice, and caps compensation for sabu' at a sheep.

Penalty for Killing a Predatory Animal by a Muhrim
Expands the ruling on sabu' penalty with rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the penalty for a Muhrim killing a sabu' and distinguish it from cases involving camels and slaves using rational legal reasoning.

Core Idea
The penalty for killing a predator in ihram is wajib, capped at one sheep, because the law does not compensate for aggression.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi position on what a Muhrim must pay if he kills a predatory animal. The ruling hinges on how the law defines a 'predator' and why the compensation is limited to a single sheep, even if the animal is valuable. The passage also clarifies boundaries by comparing with camels and slaves, sharpening our understanding of legal reasoning (istidlal).

Guided Path
1
Define the sabu' in Hanafi law
Identify the definition provided: any non-edible animal that is habitually aggressive, excluding the seven nuisances and insects. This includes pigs, monkeys, elephants, and all predators.
The definition is expansive beyond common predators.
2
Extract the ruling and its limit
State the ruling: if a Muhrim kills a sabu', compensation (jaza') is wajib, but it does not exceed one sheep. Understand the rationale: any value beyond a sheep arises from combativeness or harm, which the law does not compensate.
The cap is based on consideration of skin and meat only, assuming edibility.
3
Distinguish from the camel and slave cases
Contrast with a camel that attacks: full value must be paid because no permission to kill exists from its owner. Then address the objection of a slave attacking: no liability because the slave is guaranteed for himself (as a mukallaf), and the owner's financial interest falls as dependent.
These distinctions confirm the sabu' rule and illustrate Hanafi rational method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج/ فصل إن قتل محرم صيداً
الغنم، وأراد بالسبع كل حيوان لا يؤكل لحمه مما ليس من الفواسق السبعة والحشرات، سواء كان سبعاً أولا ولو خنزيراً أو قرداً أو فيلاً كما في المجمع والسبع اسم لكل مختطف منتهب جارح قاتل عاد عادة فإذا وجب الجزاء بقتله لا يجاوز به شاة لأن كثرة قيمته إما لما فيه من معنى المحاربة وهو خارج عن معنى الصيدية، أو لما فيه من الإيذاء وهو لا تقوم له شرعاً فبقى اعتبار الجلد واللحم على تقدير كونه مأكولاً وذلك لا يزيد على قيمة الشاة غالباً لأن الحم الشاة خير من لحم السبع. وقيد بالسبع لأن الجمل إذا صال على إنسان فقتله وجب عليه قيمته بالغة ما بلغت والفرق بينهما أن الإذن في مسألة السبع بقتله حاصل من صاحب الحق وهو الشارع . وأما في مسألة الجمل فلم يحصل الإذن من صاحبه، وأورد عليه العبد إذا صال بالسيف على إنسان فقتله المصول عليه فإنه لا يضمنه مع أنه لا إذن له أيضاً من مالكه . وأجيب بأن العبد مضمون في الأصل حقاً لنفسه بالآدمية لا للمولى لأنه مكلف كسائر المكلفين، ألا ترى أنه لو ارتد أو قتل يقتل وإذا كان مضموناً لنفسه سقط هذا الضمان بمبيح جاء من قبله وهو المصال به ومالية المولى فيه إن كانت متقومة مضمونة له نهى تبع النفس فيسقط التبع في ضمن سقوط الأصل.
Book of Hajj / Section on If a Muhrim kills game: The sheep, and he intended by 'sabu' every animal whose meat is not eaten that is not from the seven nuisances and insects, whether it is a predator or not, even if a pig, monkey, or elephant as in Al-Majma'. And 'sabu' is a name for every snatching, plundering, predatory, killing, habitually aggressive creature. When compensation is obligated by killing it, it does not exceed a sheep because the abundance of its value is either due to the meaning of combat which is outside the meaning of game, or due to the harm which is not compensated for legally. So consideration remains for skin and meat on the assumption it is edible, and that does not exceed the value of a sheep mostly, because the meat of a sheep is better than the meat of a predator. And he restricted it with 'sabu' because if a camel attacks a person and he kills it, he must pay its value whatever it may be. The difference between them is that permission in the case of the predator to kill it comes from the owner of the right, i.e., the Lawgiver. But in the case of the camel, permission from its owner has not been obtained. And it was objected concerning the slave: if he attacks with a sword a person and the attacked person kills him, then he does not guarantee it, even though there is no permission from his owner either. And it was answered that the slave is intrinsically guaranteed for himself by his humanity, not for the master, because he is obligated like other obligated persons. Do you not see that if he apostatizes or kills, he is killed? And since he is guaranteed for himself, this guarantee falls due to a permission that came from him, i.e., his attack. And the master's financial value in him, if it is valuable and guaranteed for him, is a dependent of the self, so the dependent falls within the fall of the original.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the penalty for a Muhrim killing a predatory animal. The author defines 'sabu' as any non-edible animal not among the seven nuisances or insects, including predators, pigs, monkeys, elephants. The penalty is limited to one sheep because any excess value would be due to aggression (which is not compensable) or harm (which the Law does not compensate). Only the skin and potential meat (if edible) matter, and that rarely exceeds a sheep. The qualification 'sabu' distinguishes from a camel that attacks; a camel's full value must be paid because no legal permission to kill it exists. An objection about a slave that attacks is resolved: a slave is guaranteed for himself (since he is a mukallaf), so killing him in self-defense incurs no liability; the owner's financial interest falls as dependent of the self. Thus the rule is established that for a predatory animal, the penalty is capped at one sheep.

Key Terms
سبع (sabu'): Every predatory, plundering, harmful animal that habitually attacks, excluding the seven nuisances and insects. The Hanafi definition includes even non-predators like pig, monkey, elephant if not edible.
جزاء (jaza'): Compensation for killing game while in ihram, typically a sheep or its equivalent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the maximum penalty for a Muhrim who kills a wolf (a predator)?
A) Full market value of the wolf
B) One sheep, regardless of the wolf's value
C) One camel
D) No penalty because wolves are harmful

Key Takeaway
The penalty for a Muhrim killing a sabu' is wajib and capped at one sheep, based on the principle that only harmless, edible value is compensable; aggression and harm are not.
