What a Muhrim May Slaughter: Domestic Animals
Ruling on permissibility of Muhrim slaughtering domestic animals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which domestic animals a Muhrim may slaughter and identify the condition that distinguishes permissible (domestic) from forbidden (wild) animals, applying the same principle to borderline cases like water buffalo and hybrids.

Core Idea
A Muhrim may slaughter domestic animals because they are not game.

This lesson addresses a practical question for those performing Hajj or 'Umrah: can a Muhrim slaughter domestic animals for food? The Hanafi ruling is clear: domestic animals such as sheep, cows, camels, chickens, and domestic ducks are permissible because they are not considered game. The passage then expands on the definition of 'domestic' for ducks and applies the same logic to water buffalo and hybrid offspring. Understanding this distinction is essential to avoid violating Ihram prohibitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is slaughtering (dhbh) by a Muhrim. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) for specific domestic animals.
2
List the explicitly mentioned animals
The text explicitly lists: sheep (shah), cow (baqarah), camel (ba'ir), chicken (dajajah), and domestic duck (batt ahli). These are all halal for a Muhrim to slaughter.
The restriction 'domestic' (ahli) for ducks is crucial.
3
Understand the condition: domestic vs. wild
The permissibility hinges on whether the animal is 'domestic' (not game). The text defines domestic duck as one that lives in houses and ponds and is naturally familiar (alf bi-asl al-khilqah). Wild ducks (those that fly) are game and incur a penalty (jaza').
4
Apply the same logic to water buffalo
The commentator extends the ruling to water buffalo (jawamis): in lands where they are wild (like Sudan), they are game; otherwise, they are domestic. The key is the habitual state of the animal in that region.
This shows the importance of 'urf (custom) in determining legal categories.
5
Handle hybrid offspring
The passage from Al-Majma' states: if a wild deer mates with a domestic sheep, the offspring follows the mother. Hence, killing the offspring does not require compensation because the mother (sheep) is domestic and the origin (asl).
This prevents a penalty when the mother is domestic, even if the father is wild.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمحرم ذبح شاة وبقرة وبعير ودجاجة وبط أهلي لأنها ليست بصيود وعليه إجماع الأمة. وقيد البط بالأهلي وهو الذي يكون في المساكن والحياض لأنه ألوف بأصل الخلقة احترازاً عن الذي يطير فإنه صيد فيجب الجزاء بقتله. قال الشارح فينبغي أن تكون الجواميس على هذا التفصيل فإنه في بلاد السودان وحشي ولا يعرف منه مستأنس عندهم ا هـ. وفي المجمع : ولو نزى ظبي على شاة يلحق ولدها بها يعني فلا يجب بقتل الولد جزاء لأن الأم الأصل
And for the Muhrim to slaughter sheep, cow, camel, chicken, and domestic duck is permissible because they are not game, and there is consensus of the Ummah upon it. And he restricted duck to 'domestic' which is that which is in houses and ponds because it is familiar by original creation, as a precaution against that which flies, for it is game, so compensation is obligated by killing it. The commentator said: It should be that water buffalo follow this detail, for in the lands of Sudan it is wild and no tame is known among them. And in Al-Majma': If a deer mates with a sheep, its young follows the mother, meaning no compensation is due for killing the young because the mother is the origin.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage states a clear ruling: a Muhrim may slaughter certain domestic animals because they are not considered game. These include sheep, cows, camels, chickens, and domesticated ducks. The restriction 'domestic' for ducks excludes wild ducks (which fly). The author notes that water buffalo should be treated similarly: in regions where they are wild, they are game; otherwise not. The final note from Al-Majma' covers hybrid animals: if a wild male mates with a domestic female, the offspring follows the mother, so killing it does not incur a penalty.

Key Terms
بط أهلي (batt ahli): Domestic duck that lives in houses and ponds and is familiar by nature.
جواميس (jawamis): Water buffalo.
ألوف بأصل الخلقة (alf bi-asl al-khilqah): Naturally familiar/tame by original creation.
نزى ظبي على شاة (nazā zabyun 'alā shātin): A deer mounts a sheep (i.e., mates).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A water buffalo that is tame in that region
C) A wild duck that flies
D) A sheep

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim may slaughter any domestic animal because it is not game; the key is whether the animal is naturally tame and considered domestic in that locality.

Penalty for Slaughtering Game by a Muhrim
Ruling on penalty for Muhrim slaughtering game like ring-dove or tame deer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a Muhrim must pay a penalty for slaughtering a ring-dove or a tame deer, and explain the Hanafi principle that original wildness determines game status.

Core Idea
Game status depends on original wildness, not temporary domestication.

When a person in the state of ihram kills an animal, a penalty is due if the animal is considered 'game.' But what makes an animal game? In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies that the determining factor is the animal's original creation—whether it is wild by nature—not its current state of tameness. Thus, even a ring-dove that lives near houses or a deer that has been domesticated remains game, and a Muhrim who slaughters them must pay compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
A Muhrim who slaughters a ring-dove or a tame deer must pay a penalty (jaza'). This is the legal consequence (hukm).
The penalty amount is not specified here but is detailed in other texts.
2
Understand the key principle
The criterion for being 'game' is original wildness (al-tawahhush bi-asl al-khilqah), not temporary domestication (al-ista'nis).
This principle distinguishes Hanafi from some other schools.
3
Apply to the examples
The ring-dove is originally wild and elusive by flight, even if slow to rise. The deer is originally wild though tamed. Both are game.
Temporary tameness does not change their legal category.
4
Address a potential counter-argument
The fact that the animal can be slaughtered without a chase (due to tameness) does not prove it is not game. The requirement of 'optional slaughter' was because it was previously uncatchable; now that it is catchable, that requirement falls away.
This refutes the idea that easy slaughter removes game status.
5
Note the ikhtilaf with Imam Malik
Imam Malik differs regarding the ring-dove, but the Hanafi school holds that even this ambiguous case incurs a penalty, and other cases follow by priority.
Knowing the ikhtilaf helps in comparative fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه الجزاء بذبح حمام مسرول وظبي مستأنس) لما قدمناه أن العبرة للتوحش بأصل الخلقة ولا عبرة للعارض والحمام متوحش بأصل الخلقة ممتنع بطيرانه وإن كان بطيء النهوض والاستئناس عارض، واشتراط ذكاة الاختيار لا يدل على أنه ليس بصيد لأن ذلك كان للعجز وقد زال بالقدرة عليه وفي المغرب: حمام مسرول في رجليه ريش كأنه سروايل. وإنما قيد به مع أن الحكم في الحمام مطلقاً كذلك لما أن فيه خلاف مالك وليفهم غيره بالأولى.
And upon him is the compensation for slaughtering a ring-dove and a tame deer because we have stated that the consideration is for wildness by original creation, and no regard is given to the accidental. The ring-dove is wild by original creation, elusive by its flight even if it is slow to rise, and domestication is accidental. The requirement of optional slaughter does not indicate that it is not game, because that was due to inability, and it has ceased with capability upon it. And in Al-Maghrib: 'ring-dove': on its legs are feathers like trousers. And he only restricted it with this even though the ruling for doves in general is likewise, because there is a difference of Malik, and others are understood by priority.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage states that a Muhrim who slaughters game such as a ring-dove (a type of pigeon with feathered legs) or a tame deer must pay a penalty. The principle is that the criterion for being 'game' is original wildness, not temporary domestication. The ring-dove is inherently wild, though it may become accustomed to humans. The fact that it can now be slaughtered without a chase (due to tameness) does not remove its status as game. The author mentions that Malik differs, so the restriction 'ring-dove' is to highlight that even such ambiguous cases incur penalty.

Key Terms
حمام مسرول (hamam musarwal): A ring-dove or pigeon with feathered legs, resembling trousers.
ظبي مستأنس (zhabi musta'nis): A deer that has been tamed but is originally wild.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, because its original creation is wild

D) Yes, but only half a penalty

Key Takeaway
Original wildness, not temporary domestication, determines whether an animal is game for the purposes of a Muhrim's penalty.

Muhrim's Slaughter of Game: Carrion and the Necessity Debate
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a Muhrim's slaughter of game is carrion and explain the necessity debate between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and later Hanafi authorities regarding the choice between game and carrion.

Core Idea
A Muhrim's slaughter of game is carrion; in necessity, the lesser of two prohibitions is chosen.

This passage establishes a core ruling: game slaughtered by a Muhrim is unlawful carrion, like the slaughter of a Magian. But what if the Muhrim is starving and has no other food? The Hanafi school debates whether he should eat the game (and pay a penalty) or eat ordinary carrion (which has a stronger prohibition). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply rulings in emergency situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a Muhrim slaughtering game. The ruling is that the meat becomes carrion (maytah) and is haram for everyone. The text states this absolutely, including cases of necessity.
The ruling is from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is by analogy: lawful slaughter is a lawful act; this act is unlawful, so it cannot be considered slaughter—like the slaughter of a Magian, which is also unlawful and yields carrion.
The dalil is qiyas based on the principle that an unlawful act cannot produce a lawful result.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The wajh al-dalalah (legal link) is that the prohibition of the act (slaughter by a Muhrim) prevents it from being a valid slaughter (dhakah); therefore, the meat falls under the general prohibition of carrion.
This is based on the Hanafi usul rule that a prohibited act is invalid for producing its intended legal effect.
4
Note the ikhtilaf in necessity
When the Muhrim is compelled by extreme hunger, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say he should eat the game and pay the penalty, because the prohibition of game is temporary (lifted after ihram) while carrion's prohibition is permanent. Later authorities (al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya) reportedly prefer eating carrion to avoid the penalty entirely.
The text from al-Bahr al-Raiq suggests the later fatwa is preponderant (mufta bihi).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq indicates that the view in al-Fatawa (i.e., eating carrion) is to be preferred over the view in al-Mabsut. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether the Muhrim owes a penalty or not.
This tarjih is based on the hierarchy of Hanafi authority: later fatwa works often supersede earlier positions when they represent the preferred school view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو ذبح محرم صيدا حرم) أي فهو ميتة لأن الذكاة فعل مشروع وهذا فعل حرام فلا يكون ذكاة كذبيحة المجوسي فأفاد أنه يحرم على المحرم والحلال. وأشار إلى أن الحلال لو ذبح صيد الحرم فإنه يكون ميتة أيضاً كما في غاية البيان وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المحرم الذابح مضطراً أو لا، واختلفت العبارات فيما إذا اضطر المحرم هل يذبح الصيد فيأكله أو يأكل الميتة ففي المبسوط : إنه يتناول من الصيد ويؤدي الجزاء ولا يأكل الميتة في قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف لأن حرمة الميتة أغلظ لأن حرمة الصيد ترتقع بالخروج من الإحرام أو الحرم فهي مؤقتة به بخلاف حرمة الميتة فعليه أن يقصد أخف الحرمتين دون أغلظهما، حقيقة بل حكماً مستدلاً بما يأتي من تقدير الصيد على أكل الميتة، وجعل لذلك كلام المصنف أولى من قول القدوري فهو ميتة لا يحل أكل قوله : (وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المحرم الذابح مضطراً أو لا) وكذا شمل ما لو كان مكرهاً أو مكرهاً . قال في اللباب : إذا أكره محرم محرماً على قتل صيد فعلى كل واحد منهما جزاء كامل، وإن أكره حلال محرماً فالجزاء على المحرم ولا شيء على الحلال ولو في صيد الحرم، وإن أكره محرم حلالاً على صيد إن كان في صيد الحرم فعلى المحرم جزاء كامل وعلى الحلال نصفه وإن كان في صيد الحل فالجزاء على المحرم وإن كانا حلالين في صيد الحرم إن توعده بقتل كان الجزاء على الآمر، وإن توعده بجبس كانت الكفارة على المأمور القاتل خاصة ا هـ . وبيانه في شرحه قوله : (والذي يظهر ترجيح ما في (الفتاوى أي ترجيح ما ذكره عن الفتاوى الخانية على ما قدمه عن المبسوط من أن الصيد أولى من الميتة قوله : (ويجيزه له مكفراً) يعني قال أبو يوسف:
His saying: (And if a Muhrim slaughters game, it is prohibited) i.e., it is carrion, because slaughter is a lawful act and this is an unlawful act, so it is not slaughter like the slaughter of a Magian. So it indicates that it is prohibited for both the Muhrim and the non-Muhrim. And he indicated that if a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram, it is also carrion as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he made it absolute, so it includes whether the Muhrim slaughterer is in necessity or not. And the expressions differ regarding when the Muhrim is compelled: does he slaughter game and eat it, or eat carrion? In al-Mabsut: He takes from the game and pays the penalty and does not eat carrion according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, because the prohibition of carrion is stricter. The prohibition of game is removed by leaving ihram or the Haram, so it is temporary, unlike the prohibition of carrion. So he should intend the lighter of the two prohibitions rather than the stricter, truly but as a ruling, deducing from what follows of preferring game over eating carrion. And for that, the author's statement is more appropriate than al-Quduri's statement: 'It is carrion, not permissible to eat.' His saying: 'And he made it absolute, so it includes whether the Muhrim slaughterer is in necessity or not' and likewise it includes whether he is coerced or not. He said in al-Lubab: If a Muhrim coerces another Muhrim to kill game, each owes a full penalty. If a non-Muhrim coerces a Muhrim, the penalty is on the Muhrim and nothing on the non-Muhrim even in Haram game. If a Muhrim coerces a non-Muhrim to hunt: if it is Haram game, upon the Muhrim a full penalty and upon the non-Muhrim half; if in Hal game, the penalty is on the Muhrim. If both are non-Muhrim in Haram game: if he threatens to kill, the penalty is on the commander; if he threatens imprisonment, the expiation is specifically on the commanded killer. And its explanation is in his commentary. His saying: 'And what appears is the preference of what is in al-Fatawa' i.e., preferring what he mentioned from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya over what he presented from al-Mabsut that game is preferable to carrion. His saying: 'And he permits it for him as an expiation' means Abu Yusuf said:
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage begins with the ruling that if a Muhrim slaughters game, the meat becomes carrion (maytah) and is unlawful for both Muhrim and non-Muhrim, because the act of slaughter is unlawful. The author notes that the same applies if a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram. The rule is absolute, covering even necessity (idtirar). Then a debate is presented: in extreme hunger, should the Muhrim eat the game (and pay penalty) or eat carrion (which is also prohibited)? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (in al-Mabsut) say he should eat the game and pay the penalty, because the prohibition of carrion is stronger (it is permanent) while the prohibition of game is temporary (lifts after ihram). Therefore, the lesser of two prohibitions is chosen. The author of al-Hidayah (the base text) apparently supports this. However, later fatwa (al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya) is preferred, which apparently holds differently (likely that carrion is to be eaten). The passage ends with a note about coercion scenarios and a reference to Abu Yusuf's view on allowing game as an expiation.

Key Terms
ميتة (maytah): Carrion: an animal not slaughtered according to Shari'ah; unlawful to eat.
اضطرار (idtirar): Necessity due to extreme hunger that may cause death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on the meat of game slaughtered by a Muhrim?
A) It is halal if the Muhrim is in necessity.
B) It is maytah (carrion) and haram to eat, with exceptions in necessity.
C) It is halal only for non-Muhrims.
D) It is makruh but permissible.

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim's slaughter of game renders it carrion; in necessity, the Hanafi school advises eating carrion over game to avoid the penalty, though the earlier position permits game with penalty.

Killing Domestic Animals in Ihram: No Penalty
States that killing domestic animals by a Muhrim requires no compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a Muhrim may kill domestic animals without penalty and identify the consensus (ijma') as the proof.

Core Idea
Domestic animals are exempt from game penalty for a Muhrim.

In the state of ihram, hunting game is prohibited and incurs a penalty. However, this prohibition does not extend to domestic animals. This lesson explores the ruling and its proof, including the distinction between domestic and wild varieties, and the case of hybrid offspring.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states that a Muhrim may slaughter various domestic animals (sheep, cow, camel, chicken, domestic duck) without penalty.
2
Locate the proof
The proof cited is the consensus (ijma') of the Ummah.
Consensus is a primary source of law.
3
Distinguish domestic from wild
The text qualifies duck as domestic vs. wild; only domestic duck is exempt. Wild duck is game and incurs penalty.
The criterion is original creation (asl al-khilqah).
4
Apply the principle to buffaloes
The commentator extends the distinction to buffaloes: in lands where they are wild by original nature, they are game; otherwise domestic.
Regional variation matters.
5
Apply the principle to hybrids
The offspring of a wild mountain goat and domestic sheep follows the mother, so no penalty for killing the hybrid.
The mother's ruling prevails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمحرم ذبح شاة وبقرة وبعير ودجاجة وبط أهلي لأنها ليست بصيود وعليه إجماع الأمة. وقيد البط بالأهلي وهو الذي يكون في المساكن والحياض لأنه ألوف بأصل الخلقة احترازاً عن الذي يطير فإنه صيد فيجب الجزاء بقتله. قال الشارح فينبغي أن تكون الجواميس على هذا التفصيل فإنه في بلاد السودان وحشي ولا يعرف منه مستأنس عندهم ا هـ. وفي المجمع : ولو نزى ظبي على شاة يلحق ولدها بها يعني فلا يجب بقتل الولد جزاء لأن الأم الأصل.
For the Muhrim, slaughtering a sheep, cow, camel, chicken, and domestic duck is permissible because they are not game, and upon this is the consensus of the Ummah. The duck is qualified as 'domestic' which is that which lives in houses and ponds because it is tame by original creation, as a precaution against that which flies, for it is game and compensation is obligatory for killing it. The commentator said: it is appropriate that buffaloes be considered on this detail, for in the land of Sudan it is wild and no domestic form is known among them. And in al-Majma': If a mountain goat mounts a sheep, its young follows it, meaning no compensation is obligatory for killing the young because the original mother is the basis.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining that a Muhrim (one in the state of ihram) may slaughter domestic animals without incurring the penalty (jaza') that applies to game. The proof is the consensus (ijma') of the Ummah. The text specifies that domestic duck is excluded because it is tame by original nature, whereas wild duck is game. The commentator (al-Sharh) extends the ruling to buffaloes in lands where they are wild and no domestic form is known. Additionally, a hybrid offspring of a wild mountain goat and a domestic sheep takes the ruling of the mother, so no compensation is due for killing it. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that the criterion is the original creation (asl al-khilqah) not temporary domestication.

Key Terms
جواميس (jawamis): Buffaloes, here considered wild in the Sudan and thus treated as game.
نزى (nazzā): Past tense verb meaning 'mounted' in the context of animal copulation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Killing domestic animals while in ihram incurs no penalty because they are not considered game, as established by consensus.

Compensation for Killing the Hamam Musarwal and Domesticated Antelope
Ruling statement with reasoning (talil)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that compensation (jaza') is wajib for killing the hamam musarwal and domesticated antelope, and explain the legal principle of original wildness.

Core Idea
Original wildness, not temporary domestication, determines game status — compensation is wajib even for tame animals.

In the Hajj chapter, a Muhrim who kills game (sayd) must pay compensation. But what counts as 'game'? This passage clarifies that the decisive factor is the animal's original nature, not its current behaviour. Even a pigeon with feathered legs or a tame antelope still triggers the penalty because their wildness is inherent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
The passage states that compensation (jaza') is due for slaughtering a 'hamam musarwal' and a domesticated antelope. Recognize that this is a ruling about the prohibited act of hunting during Ihram.
The ruling applies to a Muhrim; if a non-Muhrim kills them, no compensation is required.
2
Understand the key legal principle
The criterion for determining whether an animal is game (sayd) is original wildness (al-tawahhush bi-asl al-khilqah), not accidental domestication. This principle is the basis for the ruling.
The author emphasizes that domestication ('isti'nas) is an accidental ('arid) quality that does not change the legal status.
3
Apply the principle to the specific cases
The pigeon is wild by original creation, even if it becomes slow and tame. Similarly, the antelope retains its wild origin despite being domesticated. Therefore both remain game.
The text uses the phrase 'bi'l-awla' (all the more) to indicate that other similar cases are covered by this logic.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam Malik disagreed concerning the 'hamam musarwal' specifically. The Hanafi response uses the general principle to include it, and the mention of Musarwal is to address that ikhtilaf.
The disagreement shows that the ruling is not self-evident; the Hanafis rely on a broad interpretation of 'original creation'.
5
Clarify a potential confusion
Some might think that because the animal can be easily caught (due to tameness), it is not game. The passage rejects this by noting that the requirement of selective slaughter was due to inability, and once ability is regained, the animal's game status is unaffected.
This prevents a narrow reading that equates 'game' only with difficult-to-catch animals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعليه الجزاء بذبح حمام مسرول وظبي مستأنس) لما قدمناه أن العبرة للتوحش بأصل الخلقة ولا عبرة للعارض والحمام متوحش بأصل الخلقة ممتنع بطيرانه وإن كان بطيء النهوض والاستئناس عارض، واشتراط ذكاة الاختيار لا يدل على أنه ليس بصيد لأن ذلك كان للعجز وقد زال بالقدرة عليه وفي المغرب: حمام مسرول في رجليه ريش كأنه سروايل. وإنما قيد به مع أن الحكم في الحمام مطلقاً كذلك لما أن فيه خلاف مالك وليفهم غيره بالأولى.
His statement: 'and upon him is the compensation (jaza') for slaughtering a 'hamam musarwal' (pigeon with feathered legs) and a domesticated antelope' due to what we have presented: that the criterion for wildness is the original creation, and no consideration is given to the accidental. The pigeon is wild by original creation, defiant by its flight, even if it is slow to rise, and domestication is accidental. The condition of selective slaughter (dhabh al-ikhtiyar) does not indicate that it is not game, because that was due to inability and it has been removed by ability. In al-Mughrib: 'hamam musarwal' has feathers on its legs like trousers. It was qualified with this despite the ruling being general for all pigeons because of the disagreement of Malik, and to make other [cases] understood all the more.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, stating that killing a 'hamam musarwal' (a pigeon with feathered legs) and a domesticated antelope requires compensation (jaza'). The legal reasoning ('illa) is that original wildness (al-tawahhush bi-asl al-khilqah) is the criterion, not accidental domestication. The pigeon is wild by original nature, even if it becomes slow and tame. The mention of 'musarwal' is specified because Imam Malik differed on this particular type, and to make the ruling even more applicable to other cases by the principle of 'first priority' (bi'l-awla). The commentator clarifies that the requirement of selective slaughter for game does not negate its status as game, as that requirement was due to the difficulty of capturing it alive, which has been overcome.

Key Terms
حمام مسرول (hamam musarwal): A type of pigeon with feathers on its legs resembling trousers, as described in al-Mughrib.
ظبي مستأنس (zaby musta'nis): A domesticated antelope, but still ruled as game because of original wildness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a domesticated antelope considered game requiring compensation when killed by a Muhrim?
A) Because it is hard to catch
B) Because it is wild by original creation
C) Because it is a bird
D) Because it is forbidden to eat

Key Takeaway
Compensation for killing game during Ihram is based on the animal's original wild nature, not its current state of domestication.

Slaughter of Game by a Muhrim: It Becomes Carrion and the Necessity Debate
Establishes ruling then discusses preference for desperate Muhrim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a Muhrim's slaughter of game is invalid and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute over which prohibited food a desperate Muhrim should consume.

Core Idea
A Muhrim's slaughter of game yields carrion; under necessity, carrion is preferred over killing game because it involves only one prohibition.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes that when a Muhrim slaughters game, the animal becomes carrion (maytah) and is forbidden to all. It then explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement: what should a desperate (mudtar) Muhrim do when faced with starvation? Should he kill and eat game (paying the penalty) or eat carrion? The analysis applies the principle of choosing the lighter harm to resolve the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The text begins with the statement: 'If a Muhrim slaughters game, it becomes forbidden, i.e., it is carrion.' Recognize that the act is invalid because the slaughter is not a legislated act. This applies whether the Muhrim is under duress or not.
The ruling is based on the idea that valid slaughter must be a permitted act; a forbidden act cannot produce permissible meat.
2
Recognize the scope of the ruling
The author notes that the ruling is absolute, covering both the Muhrim and the non-Muhrim (if in the Haram), and also includes cases of coercion (ikrah). This sets the stage for the discussion of necessity.
The absolute nature means that even a desperate Muhrim's slaughter is invalid.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on necessity
The text presents two positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prefer carrion, while Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan prefer game. Understand the reasoning: carrion involves one prohibition (eating), game involves two (killing and eating). Also, the prohibition of game is temporary (lifted after ihram), while carrion is permanently prohibited.
The principle of akhaff al-dararayn (choosing the lighter harm) is key.
4
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The author endorses the position from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya that carrion is preferred. Note that if the game is already dead, consensus shifts to game. This shows the weight of later fatwa literature in settling intra-madhhab disputes.
The tarjih is based on counting the number of prohibited acts involved.
5
Apply to other scenarios
The text extends the reasoning to cases like coercion and comparisons with other prohibited items (usurped property, dog, pork). Understand the hierarchy of harms based on the nature of the prohibition (rights of Allah vs. rights of servants).
For example, game is preferred over usurped property because the latter involves a right of a human being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

للعبد والصيد وإن كان محظور الإحرام لكن عند الضرورة يرتفع الحظر فيقتله ويأكل منه ويؤدي الجزاء ا هـ والمراد بالقتل الذبح وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : المحرم إذا اضطر إلى مينة وصيد فالميتة أولى من قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف والحسن: يذبح الصيد، ولو كان الصيد مذبوحاً فالصيد أولى عند الكل. ولو وجد لحم صيد آدمي كان ذبح الصيد أولى، ولو وجد صيداً وكلباً فالكلب أولى لأن في الصيد ارتكاب المحظورين، وعن محمد الصيد أولى من لحم الخنزير ا هـ. والذي يظهر ترجيح ما في الفتاوى لما أن في أكل الصيد ارتكاب حرمتين الأكل والقتل وفي أكل الميتة ارتكاب حرمة واحدة وهي الأكل، وكون الحرمة ترتفع يوجب التخفيف ولهذا قال في هو بالخيار ا هـ
His statement: 'And if a Muhrim slaughters game, it becomes forbidden' i.e., it is carrion, because valid slaughter is a legislated act while this is a forbidden act, so it does not count as slaughter, like the slaughter of a Zoroastrian. Thus it indicates that it is forbidden for the Muhrim and the non-Muhrim. And he indicated that if a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram, it also becomes carrion, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. And he made it absolute, including whether the Muhrim slaughterer is under duress or not. And the expressions differ regarding when a Muhrim is forced by necessity: does he slaughter the game and eat it, or eat carrion? In al-Mabsut: he takes from the game and pays the compensation and does not eat carrion according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, because the prohibition of carrion is severer, since the prohibition of game is lifted upon leaving ihram or the Haram, so it is temporary, unlike the prohibition of carrion. So he should choose the lighter of the two prohibitions over the severer, in reality but rather in ruling, using as evidence what follows of preferring game over eating carrion. And he made the author's statement more appropriate than al-Quduri's statement that it is carrion not permissible to eat. His statement: 'and he made it absolute, including whether the Muhrim slaughterer is under duress or not' and similarly includes whether he is coerced or not. He said in al-Lubab: If a Muhrim coerces another Muhrim to kill game, each owes full compensation; if a non-Muhrim coerces a Muhrim, the compensation is on the Muhrim and nothing on the non-Muhrim, even in the Haram; if a Muhrim coerces a non-Muhrim to kill game, if it is in the Haram, the Muhrim owes full compensation and the non-Muhrim half, and if in the non-Haram, the compensation is on the Muhrim; if both are non-Muhrim in the Haram, if he threatens with death, the compensation is on the commander, if he threatens with imprisonment, the expiation is on the one who kills specifically. And its explanation is in his commentary: 'And what appears is the preference of what is in al-Fatawa' i.e., preferring what he mentioned from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya over what he presented from al-Mabsut that game is preferable to carrion. His statement: 'and he permits it for him as expiation' meaning Abu Yusuf said: not as a right of the slave, and game is otherwise prohibited in ihram, but in necessity the prohibition is lifted, so he kills it and eats from it and pays compensation. And the meaning of killing is slaughtering. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: If a Muhrim is forced to [choose between] carrion and game, then carrion is preferable according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan said: he slaughters the game. And if the game is already slaughtered, then game is preferable according to all. If he finds human flesh of game, then slaughtering game is preferable. If he finds game and a dog, the dog is preferable because in game there are two violations (killing and eating). And from Muhammad: game is preferable to pork. And what appears is the preference of what is in al-Fatawa because in eating game there is committing two prohibitions (killing and eating) while in eating carrion there is committing one prohibition (eating). And that the prohibition is lifted requires leniency, so it was said in al-Majma': carrion is preferable to game for the desperate, and he permits it for him as expiation. And it was mentioned in al-Muhit that the narration preferring carrion is the narration of al-Muntafi. And the commentator mentioned that if he finds a live game and the property of a Muslim, he eats the game, not the Muslim's property, because the game is forbidden by the right of Allah, while property is forbidden by the right of the servant, so preference is given to the right of the servant due to his need. And in Fatawa Qadikhan: from some of our companions: whoever finds the food of another, carrion is not permitted for him. Likewise from Ibn Sama'a and Bishr: usurpation is preferable to carrion. And al-Tahawi adopted this. And al-Karkhi said: he has a choice.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that if a Muhrim slaughters game, the animal becomes carrion (maytah) and is forbidden to all, because the slaughter is invalid. The commentary then delves into a complex intra-Hanafi dispute about what a desperate (mudtar) Muhrim should do when faced with starvation: should he kill and eat game, paying the penalty, or eat carrion? There are multiple positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prefer carrion over game because eating carrion involves only one prohibition (eating), while killing and eating game involves two (killing and eating). Abu Yusuf and al-Hasan prefer game, arguing that the prohibition of game is temporary and can be expiated by the penalty, whereas carrion has no expiation. The text also discusses cases of coercion (ikrah) and the preference between game and other prohibited things like usurped property or pork. The author ultimately suggests that the preferred view is from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya, which favors carrion over game for the desperate, because the prohibition is lighter and the penalty (kaffarah) can expiate it, but carrion has no expiation. The passage illustrates the Hanafi principles of choosing the lighter harm (akhaff al-dararayn) and considering the rights of Allah versus rights of servants.

Key Terms
مضطر (mudtar): A person under necessity due to hunger or threat, for whom certain prohibitions are temporarily lifted.
جزاء (jaza'): The compensation (penalty) due for killing game while in ihram; typically a sacrifice (hady) or its value.
ميتة (maytah): Carrion; an animal that died without valid slaughter, prohibited to eat.
محرم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram (ritual consecration for Hajj or Umrah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The carrion, because it involves only one prohibition
C) He can choose either
D) Neither; he must wait

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim's slaughter of game is invalid and yields carrion; under necessity, carrion is preferred over live game because it involves only one prohibited act.

Penalty on a Muhrim Eating Game Slaughtered by Another Muhrim
Explaining the penalty for eating game killed by another Muhrim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a Muhrim who eats game killed by another Muhrim and explain the legal basis for the distinction between the two Muhrims.

Core Idea
A Muhrim who eats game is penalized because he violates his ihram twice; another Muhrim only eats carrion.

The state of ihram places many restrictions on the pilgrim, including the prohibition on hunting and consuming game. But what happens when one Muhrim kills game and another Muhrim eats it? Are both liable? This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq refines the ruling by exploring the legal basis for imposing a monetary penalty on the eater, but not on a different Muhrim who merely consumes the meat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
Find the core ruling: a Muhrim who eats game slaughtered by another Muhrim must pay a penalty (ghurm). Note that this penalty is distinct from the jaza' (sacrificial penalty) owed by the slaughterer.
The penalty is the value of what he ate, not a fixed amount.
2
Distinguish the two Muhrims
The passage contrasts the slaughtering Muhrim (the one who killed the game) and the eating Muhrim (the one who consumes it). The slaughterer's ihram causes the meat to become carrion and also violates his own ihram. The eater's ihram makes the eating a separate violation.
The other Muhrim who eats does not have the additional violation of killing; he only eats carrion.
3
Understand the talil (dual vs. single prohibition)
The legal basis: the slaughterer's prohibition is from two aspects: (1) the meat is carrion, (2) eating it is a prohibited act of his ihram (since his ihram caused the invalidity). The other Muhrim only faces the carrion prohibition. Therefore, the slaughterer incurs the penalty for the additional ihram violation.
The hadith or reasoning is based on the effective cause (illa): the eater's own ihram is violated.
4
Apply the condition: before or after compensation
If the eater consumes the meat before the slaughterer has paid the compensation (jaza'), the guarantee for what he ate is included in the slaughterer's guarantee, so no separate penalty. If after, a separate penalty is owed.
This nuance shows that the penalty is not automatic regardless of timing.
5
Contrast with non-Muhrim slaughter in the Haram
The passage notes that if a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram and pays its compensation, the meat becomes permissible (not carrion). So a desperate Muhrim can eat it without penalty. This illustrates that the carrion status is key to the distinction.
This comparison reinforces that the penalty on the eater is due to the carrion nature plus the ihram violation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وغرم بأكله لا محرم (آخر) للفرق بينهما وهي أن حرمته على الذابح من جهتين : كونه ميتة وتناوله محظوراً إحرامه لأن إحرامه هو الذي أخرج الصيد عن الحلية والذابح عن الأهلية في حق الذكاة فأضيفت حرمة التناول إلى إحرامه فوجبت عليه قيمة ما أكله، وأما المحرم الآخر فإنما هي حرام عليه من جهة واحدة وهو كونه ميتة فلم يتناول محظور إحرامه ولا شيء عليه بأكل الميتة سوى التوبة والاستغفار، وبهذا اندفع قولهما بعدم الفرق قياساً على أكل الميتة أطلقه فشمل ما إذا أكل منه قبل أداء الجزاء أو بعده لكن إن كان قبله دخل ضمان ما أكل في ضمان الصيد فلا يجب له شيء بانفراده. وقيد بأكل المحرم لأن الحلال لو ذبح صيداً في الحرم فأدى جزاءه ثم أكل منه لا يجوز للمحرم المضطر أن يصيد ويأكل ويكفر وهذا أهون لأن الكفارة تجبره ولا جابر لأكل الميتة. كذا في شرح ابن الملك .
His statement: 'and he is penalized (ghurrim) by eating it, not [if eaten by] another Muhrim' due to the difference between them: that the prohibition on the slaughterer (the first Muhrim) is from two aspects: it being carrion and his consumption of a prohibited thing of his ihram, because his ihram is what removed the game from permissibility and removed the slaughterer from eligibility for valid slaughter, so the prohibition of consumption is attributed to his ihram, and therefore he owes the value of what he ate. As for the other Muhrim, it is prohibited to him from only one aspect: it being carrion, so he did not consume a prohibited thing of his ihram, and nothing is upon him for eating carrion except repentance and seeking forgiveness. And by this their statement of no difference by analogy to eating carrion is refuted. He made it absolute, covering cases where he ate before or after paying the compensation; but if it was before, the guarantee for what he ate is included in the guarantee of the game, so nothing separate is obligatory on him. And he restricted it by the eating of a Muhrim, because if a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram and pays its compensation then eats from it, it is not permissible for the desperate Muhrim to kill and eat and expiate, and that is easier because the expiation compensates it, but there is no compensator for eating carrion. Thus in Sharh Ibn al-Malak.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage refines the ruling: a Muhrim who eats from game that was killed by another Muhrim (the slaughterer) is penalized (ghurrim) and must pay the value of what he ate. This is because the eating Muhrim has committed an additional violation of his own ihram: the meat is not only carrion (due to invalid slaughter) but also a prohibited act of his ihram (eating game). In contrast, another Muhrim who merely eats from that meat without having slaughtered it incurs no such penalty; he only eats carrion, which is a sin but does not involve an additional ihram violation. The text also clarifies that if the eater Muhrim eats after the slaughterer has paid the compensation, the penalty is separate; if before, it is included in the slaughterer's overall guarantee for the game. The passage distinguishes this from the case of a non-Muhrim slaughtering in the Haram: if he pays compensation, the meat is considered permissible and not carrion, so a desperate Muhrim may eat it (with expiation for the eating? Actually the text says 'it is not permissible'? Let's re-read: 'لا يجوز للمحرم المضطر أن يصيد ويأكل ويكفر' meaning it is not permissible for the desperate Muhrim to kill and eat and expiate? The text says: 'لأن الحلال لو ذبح صيداً في الحرم فأدى جزاءه ثم أكل منه لا يجوز للمحرم المضطر أن يصيد ويأكل ويكفر وهذا أهون لأن الكفارة تجبره ولا جابر لأكل الميتة.' The translation: 'because if a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram and pays its compensation then eats from it, it is not permissible for the desperate Muhrim to kill and eat and expiate, and that is easier because the expiation compensates it, but there is no compensator for eating carrion.' The point seems to be: if the game was slaughtered by a non-Muhrim in the Haram and compensation paid, it is halal and not carrion, so a desperate Muhrim can eat it without the complications of killing; but if it is carrion (such as from an invalid slaughter by a Muhrim), then eating it has no expiation, so the desperate Muhrim should not kill game himself but rather eat the carrion? The text is ambiguous, but the main ruling here is the penalty distinction for the eater.

Key Terms
غرم (ghurim): He is required to pay a monetary penalty (gharam) equivalent to the value of the game he ate.
محظور الإحرام (mahzur al-ihram): A prohibited act specifically due to the state of ihram, such as killing game.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the eater Muhrim also committed a violation of his ihram
B) Because the first Muhrim is the owner of the game
C) Because the second Muhrim did not pay compensation
D) Because the first Muhrim ate more

Key Takeaway
A Muhrim who eats game killed by another Muhrim must pay its value because he has committed an additional ihram violation, unlike another Muhrim who only eats carrion.

Dispute on Game Slaughtered by Non-Muhrim in the Haram: Carrion or Halal?
Presents the inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute on whether game slaughtered by a non-Muhrim in the Haram becomes carrion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different Hanafi views on the ruling of game slaughtered by a non-Muhrim in the Haram and explain the practical consequence of each view.

Core Idea
The status of game slaughtered by a non-Muhrim in the Haram is disputed among Hanafis: carrion, permissible, or disliked.

In this passage, the author records a multi-layered disagreement concerning game hunted in the Haram. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq suggests that paying compensation might allow consumption, but many Hanafi authorities disagree. Additionally, the three other schools hold a stricter view. This lesson unpacks the internal Hanafi ikhtilaf and clarifies the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
What is the dispute? Is it about the permissibility of eating game slaughtered by a non-Muhrim in the Haram? Note that the matn restricts by compensation, implying a possible allowance.
2
Map the positions
List the three intra-Hanafi views: al-Karkhi says carrion (maytah), others say mubah (permissible), Qadikhan says makruh tanzih (disliked). Then note the unanimous position of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad that it is not permissible at all.
The passage only records the disagreement without giving evidence.
3
Analyze the matn's implication
The matn restricts by paying compensation, which might be interpreted as allowing consumption if paid. However, many Hanafi commentators reject this and assert it becomes carrion regardless.
This shows that later commentators often tighten the ruling.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If carrion, the meat is absolutely forbidden; if permissible, it is halal (but possibly disliked). The other schools' view also forbids it. The practical difference among Hanafi views affects whether a Muslim can eat it at all.
The passage does not give a clear preferred view (tarjih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (فأدى جزاءه ثم أكل منه التقييد بأداء الجزاء كما وقع في الفتح اتفاقي نبه عليه في النهر، ومقتضى هذا أنه ليس بميتة وهو خلاف ما مر عن غاية البيان وفي شرح اللباب : اعلم أنه صرح غير واحد كصاحب الإيضاح والبحر الزاخر والبدائع، وغيرهم بأن ذبح الحلال صيد المحرم يجعله ميتة لا يحل أكله وإن أدى جزاءه من غير تعرض لخلاف، وذكر قاضيخان أنه يكره أكله تنزيهاً . وفي اختلاف المسائل اختلفوا فيما إذا ذبح الحلال صيداً في الحرم فقال مالك والشافعي وأحمد: لا يحل أكله. واختلف أصحاب أبي حنيفة فقال الكرخي هو ميتة، وقال غيره هو مباح ا هـ . متن اللباب : إذا ذبح محرم أو حلال في الحرم صيد افذ بيحته ميتة عندنا لا يحل أكلها له ولا لغيره من
His statement: 'then he paid its compensation and then ate from it' — the restriction by paying compensation is as occurred in al-Fath coincidentally, as pointed out in al-Nahr. And the implication of this is that it is not carrion, contrary to what has passed in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in Sharh al-Lubab: Know that more than one person, such as the author of al-Idah, al-Bahr al-Zakhir, al-Bada'i', and others, stated explicitly that a non-Muhrim's slaughter of game [belonging to a Muhrim?] makes it carrion not permissible to eat even if he pays its compensation, without mentioning a disagreement. And Qadikhan mentioned that it is disliked to eat it (makruh tanzih). And in Ikhtilaf al-Masa'il: they differed regarding when a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram. Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad said: it is not permissible to eat it. And the companions of Abu Hanifa differed: al-Karkhi said it is carrion; others said it is permissible. And the text of al-Lubab: 'When a Muhrim or a non-Muhrim slaughters game in the Haram, its slaughter makes it carrion, according to us, it is not permissible for him or for anyone else to eat it...'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage discusses the disagreement over the status of game slaughtered by a non-Muhrim (halal) in the precincts of the Haram (Mecca). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq seems to indicate that there is a restriction about paying compensation, which led some to conclude that if compensation is paid, the meat becomes permissible. However, the commentators note that many Hanafi authorities (like al-Idah, al-Bahr al-Zakhir, al-Bada'i') consider it carrion regardless of compensation. Qadikhan says it is merely makruh tanzih (disliked). Then an inter-madhhab dispute is recorded: the other three schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) hold that such game is not permissible to eat at all. Among Hanafis, al-Karkhi holds it is carrion, while others say it is permissible (if compensation is paid?). The text from al-Lubab states that according to us (Hanafis), the slaughter by a Muhrim or a non-Muhrim in the Haram makes it carrion, forbidden to all. However, the earlier mention of a different view shows there is divergence even within the Hanafi school. The passage is incomplete due to page break, but it clearly presents a complex dispute.

Key Terms
الحرم (al-haram): The sanctuary area of Mecca, where special rules apply to hunting and vegetation.
ميتة (maytah): Carrion; an animal that died without valid Islamic slaughter, forbidden to eat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad regarding a non-Muhrim slaughtering game in the Haram?
A) It is permissible only if compensation is paid
B) It is not permissible to eat at all
C) It is disliked but not forbidden
D) It is carrion only if the slaughterer is a Muhrim

Key Takeaway
The ruling on game slaughtered by a non-Muhrim in the Haram is subject to significant disagreement; the dominant Hanafi view considers it carrion, but other views exist.

Ruling on a Muhrim Killing and Eating Game
Explains the ruling on a muhrim killing game and the liability for eating, including the distinction for other muhrims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a muhrim who kills game and eats from it, and distinguish the liability of the killer from that of another muhrim who eats.

Core Idea
A muhrim who kills game owes jaza for the kill and additional value for eating; another muhrim eating owes nothing extra.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the layered prohibitions a muhrim incurs when killing game. The school distinguishes between the killer, who violates both the prohibition of killing and the prohibition of consuming carrion, and another muhrim who merely eats the carrion. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying penalties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a muhrim killing game (sayd) and then eating from it. The ruling is that he must pay jaza for the kill and additionally the value (ghurm) of what he ate.
2
Distinguish the prohibitions
For the killer, the meat is carrion due to invalid slaughter, and he violates his ihram by consuming what he rendered impermissible. So two prohibitions.
The text says: 'حرمته على الذابح من جهتين'
3
Contrast another muhrim
If another muhrim eats from the same game, for him it is merely carrion. He violates only the prohibition of eating carrion, so no additional financial liability; only repentance is required.
The text states: 'فلم يتناول محظور إحرامه'
4
Note the condition of necessity
The passage later discusses forced situations, but the basic ruling applies unless necessity overrides. In necessity, the prohibition is lifted but compensation remains due.
Thamarah al-khilaf appears in Lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحج فصل إن قتل محرم صيداً لا حقاً للعبد والصيد وإن كان محظور الإحرام لكن عند الضرورة يرتفع الحظر فيقتله ويأكل منه ويؤدي الجزاء ا هـ والمراد بالقتل الذبح وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : المحرم إذا اضطر إلى مينة وصيد فالميتة أولى من قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف والحسن: يذبح الصيد، ولو كان الصيد مذبوحاً فالصيد أولى عند الكل. ولو وجد لحم صيد آدمي كان ذبح الصيد أولى، ولو وجد صيداً وكلباً فالكلب أولى لأن في الصيد ارتكاب المحظورين، وعن محمد الصيد أولى من لحم الخنزير ا هـ. والذي يظهر ترجيح ما في الفتاوى لما أن في أكل الصيد ارتكاب حرمتين الأكل والقتل وفي أكل الميتة ارتكاب حرمة واحدة وهي الأكل، وكون الحرمة ترتفع يوجب التخفيف ولهذا قال في المجمع والميتة أولى من الصيد للمضطر ويجيزه له مكفراً. وذكر في المحيط أن رواية تقديم الميتة رواية المنتفي، وذكر الشارح أنه لو وجد صيداً حياً ومال مسلم يأكل الصيد لا مال المسلم لأن الصيد حرام حقاً الله تعالى والمال حرام فكان الترجيح لحق العبد لافتقاره وفي فتاوى قاضيخان وعن بعض أصحابنا من وجد طعام الغير لا يباح له الميتة وهكذا عن ابن سماعه وبشر أن الغصب أولى من الميتة وبه أخذ الطحاوي. وقال الكرخي : هو بالخيار ا هـ قوله : (وغرم بأكله لا محرم (آخر) للفرق بينهما وهي أن حرمته على الذابح من جهتين : كونه ميتة وتناوله محظوراً إحرامه لأن إحرامه هو الذي أخرج الصيد عن الحلية والذابح عن الأهلية في حق الذكاة فأضيفت حرمة التناول إلى إحرامه فوجبت عليه قيمة ما أكله، وأما المحرم الآخر فإنما هي حرام عليه من جهة واحدة وهو كونه ميتة فلم يتناول محظور إحرامه ولا شيء عليه بأكل الميتة سوى التوبة والاستغفار، وبهذا اندفع قولهما بعدم الفرق قياساً على أكل الميتة أطلقه فشمل ما إذا أكل منه قبل أداء الجزاء أو بعده لكن إن كان قبله دخل ضمان ما أكل في ضمان الصيد فلا يجب له شيء بانفراده. وقيد بأكل المحرم لأن الحلال لو ذبح صيداً في الحرم فأدى جزاءه ثم أكل منه لا يجوز للمحرم المضطر أن يصيد ويأكل ويكفر وهذا أهون لأن الكفارة تجبره ولا جابر لأكل الميتة. كذا في شرح ابن الملك . قوله : (فأدى جزاءه ثم أكل منه التقييد بأداء الجزاء كما وقع في الفتح اتفاقي نبه عليه في النهر، ومقتضى هذا أنه ليس بميتة وهو خلاف ما مر عن غاية البيان وفي شرح اللباب : اعلم أنه صرح غير واحد كصاحب الإيضاح والبحر الزاخر والبدائع، وغيرهم بأن ذبح الحلال صيد المحرم يجعله ميتة لا يحل أكله وإن أدى جزاءه من غير تعرض لخلاف، وذكر قاضيخان أنه يكره أكله تنزيهاً . وفي اختلاف المسائل اختلفوا فيما إذا ذبح الحلال صيداً في الحرم فقال مالك والشافعي وأحمد: لا يحل أكله. واختلف أصحاب أبي حنيفة فقال الكرخي هو ميتة، وقال غيره هو مباح ا هـ . متن اللباب : إذا ذبح محرم أو حلال في الحرم صيد افذ بيحته ميتة عندنا لا يحل أكلها له ولا لغيره من محرم أو حلال، سواء اصطاده هو أي ذابحه أو غيره محرم أو حلال ولو في الحل، فلو أكل المحرم الذابح منه شيئاً قبل أداء الضمان أو بعده فعليه قيمة ما أكل ولو أكل منه غير الذابح فلا شيء عليه، ولو أكل الحلال مما ذبحه في الحرم بعد الضمان لا شيء عليه للأكل، ولو اصطاد حلال فذبح له محرم أو اصطاد محرم فذبح له حلال فهو ميتة ا هـ . قوله : (وقد قدمناه أي تحت قول المتن وهو قيمة الصيد في مقتله قوله : ( لأن الهبة كانت فاسدة رأيت بخط بعض الفضلاء هذا مبني على أن الهبة الفاسدة لا تفيد الملك، وأما على مقابله فلا شيء عليه كما نقله العلائي فراجعه ا هـ . قلت : وفيه أن الهبة هنا باطلة لا يملكها الموهوب له لأن العين خرجت عن المحلية لسائر التصرفات كما
Book of Hajj, chapter: If a muhrim kills game... He incurs liability for eating it, not another muhrim. The distinction is that its prohibition on the slaughterer is from two aspects: it being carrion and his consuming what is prohibited by his ihram. As for another muhrim, it is prohibited to him from one aspect only: it being carrion, so he did not consume what is prohibited by his ihram, and nothing is due from him for eating carrion except repentance and seeking forgiveness...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion of the ruling on a muhrim who kills game. The core ruling: if a muhrim kills game (sayd), he must pay compensation (jaza) for the kill, and if he eats from it, he must additionally pay the value of what he ate (ghurm). Another muhrim who eats from the same game is not liable for that value because for him the meat is merely carrion (maytah) — prohibited but only from one aspect, whereas the killer faces a double prohibition: the carrion aspect plus violating his own ihram by consuming what he himself rendered impermissible. The passage then discusses the case of necessity: when a muhrim is forced to eat, is carrion or game preferable? The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that carrion is better because it involves only one prohibition (eating) whereas game involves two (killing and eating). There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad favor carrion, while Abu Yusuf and Hasan favor slaughtering game. The text also covers the case of a non-muhrim (halal) slaughtering game in the Haram: according to the Hanafi school, that game becomes carrion and is not permissible to eat even after paying compensation, but there is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars whether it is absolutely forbidden or merely makruh. The hadith of Abu Qatada is cited to show that a muhrim may eat game hunted and slaughtered by a halal person for himself, provided the muhrim did not direct or indicate the hunt. If he did, it is forbidden. The passage concludes with a note on a gift of game between muhrims: if a muhrim gives game to another muhrim, the gift is invalid (fāsid), leading to liability for the killer and potentially for the recipient depending on the view on invalid gifts.

Key Terms
محرِم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah, subject to prohibitions including killing game.
صيد (sayd): Wild game that is permissible to hunt when not in ihram; its hunting is prohibited for a muhrim.
جزاء (jaza'): The compensation (penalty) due from a muhrim who kills game, either by sacrificing a similar animal, feeding the poor, or fasting.
ميتة (maytah): Carrion, i.e., an animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter; impermissible to eat except in necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a muhrim (A) kills a game and another muhrim (B) eats from it, what is the liability of (B)?
A) He must pay the full value of what he ate.
B) He must pay half the value.
C) He is not liable for any monetary compensation, but must repent.
D) He must pay the penalty for killing game.

Key Takeaway
The killer muhrim bears double liability; another muhrim eating from the same game bears only the prohibition of carrion.

Forced Situation: Preference between Carrion and Game
Explains the intra-Hanafi dispute on the forced muhrim's preference between carrion and game.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi preference between carrion and game for a forced muhrim, and state the intra-Hanafi disagreement.

Core Idea
When forced by hunger, a muhrim should prefer carrion over game because it involves fewer prohibitions.

Building on the previous lesson, this passage addresses a critical scenario: a muhrim in dire need must decide between carrion and game. The Hanafi school gives guidance based on minimizing legal violations, but there is a split among the imams. The relied-upon view favors carrion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the forced scenario
The text discusses a muhrim who is forced by hunger and finds both carrion and game. The question is which he should consume.
The term 'مضطر' is used.
2
State the two positions
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, carrion is preferred. According to Abu Yusuf and Hasan, slaughtering game is preferred.
In فتاوى قاضيخان: المحرم إذا اضطر إلى مينة وصيد فالميتة أولى من قول أبي حنيفة ومحمد وقال أبو يوسف والحسن: يذبح الصيد.
3
Analyze the reasoning for carrion
Carrion involves only one prohibition (eating), while game involves two (killing and eating). Even though both prohibitions are lifted in necessity, the one with fewer original prohibitions is lighter.
فإن في أكل الصيد ارتكاب حرمتين الأكل والقتل وفي أكل الميتة ارتكاب حرمة واحدة
4
Examine the opposing view
The opponents argue that game is fresher and the prohibition of killing can be expiated by paying compensation.
The response: compensation does not remove the double violation.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author and the majority of later Hanafis prefer carrion, as stated in Al-Majmu' and Al-Muhit.
والذي يظهر ترجيح ما في الفتاوى

Another muhrim who eats from the same game is not liable for that value because for him the meat is merely carrion (maytah) — prohibited but only from one aspect, whereas the killer faces a double prohibition: the carrion aspect plus violating his own ihram by consuming what he himself rendered impermissible. The passage then discusses the case of necessity: when a muhrim is forced to eat, is carrion or game preferable? The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that carrion is better because it involves only one prohibition (eating) whereas game involves two (killing and eating). There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad favor carrion, while Abu Yusuf and Hasan favor slaughtering game. The text also covers the case of a non-muhrim (halal) slaughtering game in the Haram: according to the Hanafi school, that game becomes carrion and is not permissible to eat even after paying compensation, but there is a difference of opinion among Hanafi scholars whether it is absolutely forbidden or merely makruh. The hadith of Abu Qatada is cited to show that a muhrim may eat game hunted and slaughtered by a halal person for himself, provided the muhrim did not direct or indicate the hunt. If he did, it is forbidden. The passage concludes with a note on a gift of game between muhrims: if a muhrim gives game to another muhrim, the gift is invalid (fāsid), leading to liability for the killer and potentially for the recipient depending on the view on invalid gifts.

Key Terms
محرِم (muhrim): A person in the state of ihram for Hajj or Umrah, subject to prohibitions including killing game.
صيد (sayd): Wild game that is permissible to hunt when not in ihram; its hunting is prohibited for a muhrim.
جزاء (jaza'): The compensation (penalty) due from a muhrim who kills game, either by sacrificing a similar animal, feeding the poor, or fasting.
ميتة (maytah): Carrion, i.e., an animal that died without proper Islamic slaughter; impermissible to eat except in necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position, what should a forced muhrim choose?
A) Carrion
B) Game
C) Either is equally good
D) Neither, he should fast instead

Key Takeaway
In necessity, carrion is preferable to game for a muhrim because it entails fewer prohibitions.
